The Three Cardinal Virtues and the Mean
The author defines iffah, shaja'ah, and hikmah as means between extremes for each power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the three cardinal virtues of iffah, shaja'ah, and hikmah are defined as means between extremes for each of the three powers, and how their combination constitutes justice.

Core Idea
Moral virtue is the golden mean for each power; their harmony is justice.

This passage builds on the three powers (appetitive, irascible, rational) by defining the specific virtue for each as a balanced state between two vices. It then reveals that these three virtues are not separate but together form the comprehensive virtue of justice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three virtues and powers
Read the passage and locate the three virtues: chastity (iffah), courage (shaja'ah), and wisdom (hikmah). Note which power each virtue corresponds to: appetitive, irascible, and practical intellectual, respectively.
The powers were introduced in a previous lesson.
2
Find the two vices for each virtue
For each virtue, the passage mentions a vice of excess and a vice of deficiency. Write them down: for iffah: fujur (excess) and khumud (deficiency); for shaja'ah: tahawwur (excess) and jubn (deficiency); for hikmah: jarbaza (excess) and balaha (deficiency).
3
Understand the mean concept
Notice that each virtue is described as 'mutawassiṭa' (intermediate) between two extremes. This reflects the Aristotelian idea of virtue as a mean, adopted in Islamic ethics.
The mean is not mediocrity but a balanced state.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by stating that these three means are the roots of all moral virtues, and their sum is called justice. Consider how each virtue contributes to overall justice in character.
Justice here is comprehensive virtue, not just a legal concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالعفة هي هيئة للقوة الشهوية متوسطة بين الفجور الذي هو إفراط هذه القوة والخمود الذي هو تفريطها، والشجاعة هيئة للقوة الغضبية متوسطة بين التهور الذي هو إفراط في هذه القوة والجبن الذي هو تفريط فيها، والحكمة هيئة للقوة العقلية العملية متوسطة بين الجربزة إفراط هذه القوة والبلاهة التي هي تفريطها، فهذه الأوساط الثلاثة أصول الفضائل الخلقية، ومجموعها يسمى عدالة ومقابل العدالة شيء واحد هو الجور
So chastity is a quality of the appetitive power, intermediate between profligacy, which is excess of this power, and insensibility, which is deficiency of it. And courage is a quality of the irascible power, intermediate between recklessness, which is excess in this power, and cowardice, which is deficiency in it. And wisdom is a quality of the practical intellectual power, intermediate between knavery, which is excess of this power, and stupidity, which is deficiency of it. So these three means are the roots of the moral virtues, and their sum is called justice, and the opposite of justice is a single thing, which is injustice.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage specifies the three cardinal virtues corresponding to the three powers introduced earlier. For the appetitive power (shahwiyya), the virtue is chastity ('iffa), which is the mean between profligacy (fujur) and insensibility (khumud). For the irascible power (ghadabiyya), the virtue is courage (shaja'a), which is the mean between recklessness (tahawwur) and cowardice (jubn). For the practical intellectual power ('amaliyya, not theoretical), the virtue is wisdom (hikma), which is the mean between knavery (jarbaza) and stupidity (balaha). The author then states that these three means are the roots of all moral virtues, and their combination is called justice ('adala). The opposite of justice is a single thing: injustice (jawr). Thus, moral character is ultimately about achieving balance in each faculty, and the harmonious integration of these virtues constitutes justice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the three virtues are independent and unrelated to each other.
Correction: The passage states that the three means are the roots of all moral virtues, and their sum is justice. They are interconnected and together constitute comprehensive virtue.

Checkpoint
What is the vice opposite to the virtue of wisdom (hikma) on the side of excess?
A) Balaha (stupidity)
B) Jarbaza (knavery)
C) Fujur (profligacy)
D) Jubn (cowardice)

Key Takeaway
The three virtues (chastity, courage, wisdom) are the balanced states of the three powers, and their unity is justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about a recent conflict or disagreement. Which of the three powers (appetitive, irascible, intellectual) was not in balance for you? Were you leaning toward excess or deficiency? What would the mean look like in that situation, and what is one concrete action you can take this week to move toward it?
?
The passage says that the sum of chastity, courage, and wisdom is justice. Name a relationship where you often act unjustly. Which of these three virtues are you lacking in that relationship? Be honest: are you indulging your desires (lack of chastity), avoiding confrontation (lack of courage), or acting foolishly (lack of wisdom)? Commit to a specific change.
?
You cannot be just without all three virtues working together. Right now, which of these three do you most rely on, and which do you neglect? How has that neglect affected your character? What is one step you can take this week to strengthen the neglected virtue?

The Three Cardinal Virtues as Means Between Extremes
The author enumerates the three cardinal virtues—chastity, courage, wisdom—as means between their respective extremes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three cardinal moral virtues as means between extremes of excess and deficiency, and how they together constitute justice.

Core Idea
Every moral virtue is a middle state between two vices.

The author lays out the ethical framework that underlies much of Islamic moral theology. Here he enumerates the three foundational virtues derived from the soul's powers, each balanced between excess and deficiency. Let's see how these virtues form the basis of justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's enumeration and classification come through.
2
Identify the three powers of the soul
The passage mentions three powers: appetitive (shahwiyya), irascible (ghadabiyya), and practical rational (ʿaqliyya ʿamaliyya). Note each power's virtue.
3
For each power, note the virtue and its extremes
Chastity (ʿiffa) is between profligacy (fujūr) and insensibility (khumūd). Courage (shajāʿa) between recklessness (tahawwur) and cowardice (jubn). Wisdom (ḥikma) between cunning (jarbaza) and dullness (balāha).
4
Understand the totality as justice
The three virtues together are called justice (ʿadāla), and its opposite is injustice (jawr). Reflect on why this classification is comprehensive.
5
Reflect on the mean doctrine
Ask yourself: Why are both extremes blameworthy? How does this relate to the broader ethical tradition?
This doctrine is often traced to Aristotle's Nicomachean Ethics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالفضائل الخلقية أصولها ثلاثة هي الأوساط من أحوال القوى المذكورة. والرذائل الخلقية أصولها ستة هي أطراف تلك الأوساط: ثلاثة منها من قبيل الإفراط وثلاثة أخرى من قبيل التفريط. كلا طرفي كل الأمور ذميم. فالعفة هي هيئة للقوة الشهوية متوسطة بين الفجور الذي هو إفراط هذه القوة والخمود الذي هو تفريطها. والشجاعة هيئة للقوة الغضبية متوسطة بين التهور الذي هو إفراط في هذه القوة والجبن الذي هو تفريط فيها. والحكمة هيئة للقوة العقلية العملية متوسطة بين الجربزة إفراط هذه القوة والبلاهة التي هي تفريطها. فهذه الأوساط الثلاثة أصول الفضائل الخلقية، ومجموعها يسمى عدالة. ومقابل العدالة شيء واحد هو الجور.
So the moral virtues, their foundations are three: they are the means among the states of the mentioned powers. And the moral vices, their foundations are six: they are the extremes of those means: three of them are of the type of excess and three others are of the type of deficiency. Both extremes of all things are blameworthy. Thus chastity (ʿiffa) is a disposition for the appetitive power, a mean between profligacy (fujūr), which is the excess of this power, and insensibility (khumūd), which is its deficiency. And courage (shajāʿa) is a disposition for the irascible power, a mean between recklessness (tahawwur), which is an excess in this power, and cowardice (jubn), which is a deficiency in it. And wisdom (ḥikma) is a disposition for the practical rational power, a mean between cunning (jarbaza), the excess of this power, and dullness (balāha), which is its deficiency. So these three means are the foundations of the moral virtues, and their totality is called justice (ʿadāla). And the opposite of justice is one thing: injustice (jawr).
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage enumerates the three cardinal moral virtues as the means (awsaṭ) of the three powers of the soul. For each power, virtue is the middle state between two vices: one of excess (ifrāṭ) and one of deficiency (tafrīṭ). For the appetitive power, the virtue is chastity (ʿiffa), between profligacy (fujūr) and insensibility (khumūd). For the irascible power, the virtue is courage (shajāʿa), between recklessness (tahawwur) and cowardice (jubn). For the practical rational power, the virtue is wisdom (ḥikma), between cunning (jarbaza) and dullness (balāha). The three means together constitute justice (ʿadāla), whose opposite is injustice (jawr). This passage is a clear statement of the Aristotelian-influenced ethical theory adopted by many Muslim theologians, placing virtue in the mean.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Wisdom in this passage refers to theoretical wisdom (theoretical intellect).
Correction: The passage specifies wisdom as a disposition of the practical rational power, not the theoretical intellect.

Checkpoint
What is the virtue corresponding to the irascible power?
A) Chastity
B) Courage
C) Wisdom
D) Justice

Key Takeaway
The three cardinal virtues—chastity, courage, and wisdom—are each a mean between two vices, and together they constitute justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of your own desires is most prone to either excess (profligacy) or deficiency (insensibility)? What specific step will you take this week to bring it toward the mean of chastity?
?
Recall a recent situation where you acted with either recklessness or cowardice. What would true courage—the mean—have looked like in that exact moment, and what stopped you from embodying it?
?
Justice here is the harmony of all three virtues. In which area of your life are you most out of balance—are you neglecting wisdom, courage, or chastity? What is one concrete change you can make today to restore that balance?

Distinguishing the Virtue of Wisdom from Theoretical-Practical Wisdom
Refutation of confusing virtuous wisdom with theoretical-practical wisdom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the wisdom that is a moral virtue is distinct from the wisdom that is divided into theoretical and practical, and why this distinction matters in Islamic ethics and theology.

Core Idea
Virtue wisdom is a balanced faculty; theoretical wisdom is knowledge of all things.

In this passage, the author corrects a common mistake: thinking that the wisdom praised as a moral virtue is the same as the wisdom that philosophers divide into theoretical and practical. He explains that one is a faculty (malaka) for right action, the other is knowledge (ʿilm) of truths. Understanding this difference is crucial for proper moral and intellectual development.

Guided Path
1
Read the full Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Let the author's argument unfold.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the mistaken view
The author mentions a false notion—someone thought wisdom here (the virtue) is the same as wisdom divided into theoretical and practical. Find that.
This is the opening point.
3
Distinguish between faculty and knowledge
The author says the virtue wisdom is a malaka producing moderate actions, while practical wisdom (in the division) is ʿilm about our actions. Compare them.
This is the core distinction.
4
Understand the scope of excess
The author notes that blameworthy excess only applies to practical reason, not theoretical. Why? Because theoretical reason is better when stronger.
This highlights the different nature of the two powers.
5
Reflect on the superiority of theoretical wisdom
The author concludes that justice (composed of virtues) is not superior to theoretical wisdom, which is knowledge of God and realities. Connect this back to the main distinction.
This shows the hierarchy of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الملخص، قد ظن بعضهم أن الحكمة المذكورة هاهنا هي التي جعلت قسيمة للحكمة النظرية حيث قيل: الحكمة إما نظرية وإما عملية، وهو ظن باطل إذ المقصود من هذه الحكمة ملكة تصدر عنها أفعال متوسطة بين أفعال الجريزة والغباوة، والمراد بتلك الحكمة العملية العلم بالأمور التي وجودها من أفعالنا، والفرق بين العلم المذكور والملكة المذكورة معلوم بالضرورة. وقد تبين مما نقلناه أيضاً أن الحكمة المذكورة هاهنا مغايرة للحكمة التي قسمت إلى النظرية والعملية لأنها بمعنى العلم بالأشياء مطلقاً سواء كانت مستندة إلى قدرتنا أو لا. ومما يجب التنبه له أن الإفراط المذموم إنما يتصور في القوة العقلية العملية دون النظرية، فإن هذه القوة أعني النظرية كلما كانت أشد وأقوى كانت أفضل وأعلى، وأن العدالة المركبة من الحكمة والعفة والشجاعة والحكمة تكون أفضل من كل واحدة من أجزائها لا النظرية، إذ لا كمال أشرف من معرفته تعالى بصفاته ومعرفة أفعاله في المبدأ والمعاد والاطلاع على حقائق مخلوقاته وأحوالها وليست هذه داخلة في العدالة كما يظهر بأدنى تأمل في مقالتهم لمن له فطرة سليمة.
And in al-Mulakhkhaṣ, some thought that the wisdom mentioned here is that which was made a counterpart to theoretical wisdom, where it was said: wisdom is either theoretical or practical, and it is a false notion because the intended meaning of this wisdom is a faculty from which proceed actions that are a mean between the actions of cunning and stupidity, and the intended meaning of that practical wisdom is knowledge of matters whose existence is from our actions, and the difference between the mentioned knowledge and the mentioned faculty is known necessarily. And it has also become clear from what we have transmitted that the wisdom mentioned here is different from the wisdom that was divided into theoretical and practical because it is in the sense of knowledge of things absolutely, whether they are dependent on our power or not. And among things that must be noted is that blameworthy excess is only conceivable in the practical rational power, not in the theoretical, for this power, I mean the theoretical, the stronger and more powerful it is, the better and higher it is, and that justice composed of wisdom, chastity, courage, and wisdom is better than each of its parts, but not theoretical wisdom, since there is no perfection nobler than knowledge of Him, may He be exalted, with His attributes, and knowledge of His actions in the beginning and the return, and insight into the realities of His creatures and their states, and these are not included in justice, as appears with minimal reflection on their statements for one with a sound disposition.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage refutes a mistaken view that the wisdom (ḥikma) which is a moral virtue is the same as the wisdom that is divided into theoretical and practical. The author argues that the virtue of wisdom is a faculty (malaka) producing actions that are a mean between cunning and dullness, whereas practical wisdom (in the division) is knowledge of matters within our power. The difference between knowledge (ʿilm) and faculty (malaka) is obvious. Furthermore, the wisdom mentioned here is different from wisdom as knowledge of things absolutely (whether within our power or not). The author also notes that blameworthy excess applies only to the practical rational power, not the theoretical—theoretical virtue increases in excellence with strength. Justice (ʿadāla), composed of the three virtues, is superior to each of its parts, but not superior to theoretical wisdom, which is the noblest perfection: knowledge of God, His attributes, and His actions in creation and the afterlife, as well as insight into the realities of creatures. These lofty matters are not included in justice, as is clear to anyone of sound disposition. This passage carefully delineates the scope of moral virtues against intellectual virtues.

Key Terms
الحكمة
الحكمة النظرية
الحكمة العملية
الملكة
الإفراط
التفريط
العدالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The wisdom praised as a moral virtue is the same as the wisdom that philosophers divide into theoretical and practical.
Correction: The passage clarifies that virtuous wisdom is a faculty (malaka) for producing moderate actions, whereas practical wisdom (in the division) is knowledge (ʿilm) of our actions, and theoretical wisdom is absolute knowledge. They are distinct in nature and scope.

Checkpoint
Why is the wisdom that is a virtue different from theoretical wisdom?
A) Theoretical wisdom is a faculty for practical actions
B) The virtue wisdom is a faculty producing mean actions, while theoretical wisdom is knowledge of absolute truths
C) They are the same but called differently
D) Theoretical wisdom is blameworthy if excessive

Key Takeaway
The wisdom that is a virtue is a faculty for balanced action, distinct from both theoretical and practical wisdom as types of knowledge; theoretical wisdom, being knowledge of God and realities, is the highest perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you often mistake knowing about virtue for actually being virtuous? In what specific area of your life has this gap between knowledge and action been most damaging? What will you change today to align your actions with what you know?
?
The passage says theoretical wisdom—knowledge of God and His attributes—has no limit; it only increases in value. Are you pursuing that knowledge with seriousness, or are you satisfied with a superficial understanding? What concrete step can you take this week to deepen your knowledge of God?
?
If justice composed of virtues is good but not as good as theoretical wisdom of God, where is the focus of your efforts? Are you more concerned with being seen as just and balanced, or with knowing the Creator? How would your daily priorities shift if you truly believed knowing God is the highest perfection?

Distinction Between Character and Ability
The author distinguishes character from ability using three criteria.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key differences between character (khuluq) and ability (qudra) in Islamic kalam, especially regarding ease, temporal relation to the act, and relation to opposites.

Core Idea
Character is not the same as ability—it differs in ease, timing, and direction.

In this passage, the author clarifies a subtle distinction between two key terms in kalam: character (khuluq) and ability (qudra). While both relate to human actions, they differ in important conditions. Let's read and unpack the reasons why they are considered separate concepts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Notice the three differences the author mentions.
The passage is concise; each difference is signaled by 'because', 'also', and 'especially'.
2
Identify the three distinctions
List the three criteria that differentiate character from ability: (1) ease without deliberation, (2) simultaneity with the act (for ability according to Ash'aris), (3) relation to opposites.
You may want to annotate the Arabic text with these points.
3
Reflect on theological significance
Consider why these differences matter. Character is a disposition, while ability is a power that can be equally directed toward opposites.
This distinction helps in discussions of human responsibility and divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخلق مغاير للقدرة لأن الخلق يعتبر فيه صدور الأفعال بسهولة من غير تقدم روية وليس يعتبر ذلك في أصل القدرة. وأيضاً لا يجب في الخلق أن يكون مع الفعل كما وجب ذلك عند الأشاعرة في القدرة، فالفرق بينهما ظاهر. سيما إن جعلت نسبة القدرة إلى الطرفين على السواء، فإن الخلق لا يتصور فيه ذلك، بل لا بد أن يكون متعلقاً بأحد طرفي الفعل واحد من الضدين.
And character (khuluq) is different from ability (qudra) because in character, the occurrence of actions with ease without prior deliberation is considered, and that is not considered in the essence of ability. Also, it is not necessary for character to be with the act as that was necessary, according to the Ashʿarīs, in ability, so the difference between them is apparent. Especially if the relation of ability to both alternatives is made equal, for character cannot be conceived in that [i.e., being equally related to opposites]; rather, it must be related to one of the two alternatives of the act, one of the two opposites.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage argues that character (khuluq) is distinct from ability (qudra). First, character implies that actions issue with ease (suhūla) and without prior deliberation (taraḍḍī), whereas ability does not require these conditions. Second, according to the Ashʿarīs, ability must accompany the act simultaneously, but this is not required for character. Third, ability is equally related to opposites (e.g., between doing and refraining), but character is not neutral—it is naturally inclined toward one specific type of action (virtue or vice). This distinction is important in kalam to differentiate between innate dispositions and the power to act, which is a separate divine attribute.

Key Terms
الخلق
القدرة
السهولة
الروية
الأشاعرة
الضدان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Character (khuluq) is just another name for ability (qudra) to act.
Correction: Character has specific conditions (ease, no deliberation, inclination to one opposite) that are not required for ability, and ability has its own conditions (simultaneity with act according to Ash'aris, equal relation to opposites) that are not shared by character.

Checkpoint
What is a key difference between character (khuluq) and ability (qudra) according to this passage?
A) Character requires deliberation, ability does not
B) Character involves ease without deliberation, while ability does not require that
C) Ability is always virtuous, character can be vicious
D) Character is not related to actions

Key Takeaway
Character is distinct from ability because it involves ease, lacks a requirement of simultaneity with the act, and is directed toward one opposite rather than being neutral.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an action you performed recently with genuine ease, without hesitation or deliberation. Did that ease come from a deeply ingrained character trait? How does this lesson's distinction between character and ability make you re-evaluate the source of that action? Will you now examine whether your easy virtues are truly part of your character or just momentary abilities?
?
Consider an area of your life where you feel you have ability but not character—for example, you can pray regularly but it feels forced. According to this lesson, ability alone does not guarantee ease or inclination. Are you relying on your ability while neglecting the cultivation of character? What would it take for that action to become part of your character through consistent practice and sincere intention?
?
The passage states that character is directed toward one opposite—either good or evil. Can you identify a habit you have that is neutral in ability but tends toward a particular moral direction? Are you consciously steering your character toward the good, or are you letting your inclinations drift? What concrete step will you take today to align your character more firmly with what pleases Allah?

Khuluq Distinguished from Qudra
Al-Taftazani argues that khuluq is distinct from qudra and refutes a potential objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani distinguishes character (khuluq) from power (qudra) using two rational proofs.

Core Idea
Khuluq requires ease and is one-sided; qudra is neutral and concurrent.

After clarifying the nature of wisdom, the author turns to a critical distinction: character versus power. While both relate to actions, they differ in essential features such as ease of issuance and relation to opposites. This lesson examines the precise arguments that establish their separation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
2
Identify the two reasons for distinction
The author gives two clear reasons: (1) khuluq requires ease of action without prior deliberation, (2) khuluq is not necessarily concurrent with the act. Find these in the text.
3
Note the special case of power attributed to both sides
The author adds that if power is equally related to both opposites, khuluq cannot be like that; it must be confined to one side.
4
Consider the refutation of an objection
The passage ends by addressing an objection about khuluq attaching to standing and sitting, which are opposites. Understand why a proof is needed to negate that.

للحكمة النظرية حيث قيل : الحكمة إما نظرية وإما عملية وهو ظن باطل إذ المقصود من هذه الحكمة ملكة تصدر عنها أفعال متوسطة بين أفعال الجريزة والغباوة، والمراد بتلك الحكمة العملية العلم بالأمور التي وجودها من أفعالنا، والفرق بين العلم المذكور والملكة المذكورة معلوم بالضرورة، وقد تبين مما نقلناه أيضاً أن الحكمة المذكورة هاهنا مغايرة للحكمة التي قسمت إلى النظرية والعملية لأنها بمعنى العلم بالأشياء مطلقاً سواء كانت مستندة إلى قدرتنا أو لا، ومما يجب التنبه له أن الافراط المذموم، إنما يتصور في القوة العقلية العملية دون النظرية، فإن هذه القوة أعني النظرية كلما كانت أشد وأقوى كانت أفضل وأعلى، وأن العدالة المركبة من الحكمة العفة والشجاعة والحكمة تكون أفضل من كل واحدة من أجزائها لا النظرية، إذ لا كمال أشرف من معرفته تعالى بصفاته ومعرفة أفعاله في المبدأ والمعاد والاطلاع على حقائق مخلوقاته وأحوالها وليست هذه داخلة في العدالة كما يظهر بأدنى تأمل في مقالتهم لمن له فطرة سليمة ( والخلق مغاير للقدرة ) لأن الخلق يعتبر فيه صدور الأفعال بسهولة من غير تقدم روية وليس يعتبر ذلك في أصل القدرة وأيضاً، لا يجب في الخلق أن يكون مع الفعل كما وجب ذلك عند الأشاعرة في القدرة فالفرق بينهما ظاهر ( سيما إن جعل تسبة القدرة إلى الطرفين على السواء ) فإن الخلق لا يتصور فيه ذلك، بل لا بد أن يكون متعلقاً بأحد طرفي الفعل واحد من الضدين.
قوله : ( مغايرة للحكمة إلخ رد لما في شرح المقاصد حيث قال : والحديث من الاعتدال حركة المفصلة الحكمة وهي معرفة الحقائق على ما هي عليه بقدر الاستطاعة . قوله : (من الحكمة (النظرية المعرفة بمعرفة حقائق الموجودات التي ليست وجودها بقدرتنا واختيارنا . قوله : ( وليست هذه داخلة إلخ فإن الداخلة فيها بمعنى ملكة يحصل من استعمال الفكر على ما ينبغي كما عرفت . قوله : (بل لا بد إلخ) أو سهولة صدور العارضين والضدين بقياس كل منهما إلى الآخر لا بتصور كيفيات نفسانية ولو باعتبار بعض المعاني فذكر جميع ماله المعاني مقصود في القائمة لا كما وهم من أن ذكر تفسير محبته تعالى استطرادي . قوله : ( فإن الخلق لا يتصور فيه ذلك قد بناقش فيه بجواز تعلق الخلق بالقيام والقعود مثلاً مع أنهما ضدان لا بد لنفيه من دليل.
Regarding theoretical wisdom: it is said that wisdom is either theoretical or practical, and this is a false assumption because the intended meaning of this wisdom is a faculty from which actions intermediate between the actions of instinct and stupidity issue. And what is meant by practical wisdom is knowledge of things whose existence is from our actions. The difference between the aforementioned knowledge and the aforementioned faculty is known necessarily. And it has become clear from what we have narrated that the wisdom mentioned here is different from the wisdom that was divided into theoretical and practical because it means knowledge of things absolutely, whether they are dependent on our power or not. And among the things that must be noted is that blameworthy excess is only conceivable in the practical rational faculty, not the theoretical. For this faculty—I mean the theoretical—the stronger and more powerful it is, the better and higher it is. And that justice composed of wisdom, chastity, and courage is better than each of its parts, but not theoretical wisdom, for there is no perfection nobler than knowledge of Him, exalted, with His attributes, and knowledge of His actions in the beginning and the end, and insight into the realities of His creatures and their states. And these are not included in justice, as appears with the slightest reflection on their discourse for one with a sound disposition. (And character is different from power) because in character, the issuance of actions with ease without prior deliberation is considered, but that is not considered in the principle of power. Also, it is not necessary in character that it be with the act, as was necessary according to the Ash'aris regarding power; thus the difference between them is apparent. (Especially if power is attributed to both sides equally) for that is not conceivable in character; rather, it must be related to one of the two sides of the act, one of the two opposites. His saying: (Different from wisdom, etc.) is a refutation of what is in Sharh al-Maqasid where he said: “And the hadith from moderation is the movement of the joint? Wisdom is knowledge of realities as they are to the extent of ability.” His saying: (From theoretical wisdom is knowledge of the realities of existents whose existence is not by our power and choice.) His saying: (And these are not included, etc.) for what is included in it is in the sense of a faculty obtained from using thought as it should be, as you have known. His saying: (Rather, it is necessary, etc.) or the ease of the emergence of the accidents and opposites by comparing each to the other, not by conceiving psychological qualities, even if by considering some meanings. So mentioning all that has meanings is intended in the list, not as is imagined that mentioning the interpretation of love of Him, exalted, is digressive. His saying: (For character is not conceivable in that) has been discussed by allowing the attachment of character to standing and sitting, for example, while they are two opposites, so a proof is needed for its negation.
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage is a proof and refutation. The author first corrects a misunderstanding about wisdom (hikma): contrary to the claim that wisdom is either theoretical or practical, he explains that the wisdom here is a faculty (malaka) producing intermediate actions. He distinguishes practical wisdom (knowledge of our own actions) from the faculty itself. Then he argues that the wisdom under discussion is different from the division into theoretical and practical because it is knowledge of all things absolutely. He notes that blameworthy excess applies only to practical rationality, not theoretical, which is always better when stronger. Justice composed of wisdom, chastity, and courage is superior to its parts, but not to theoretical wisdom, which is the highest perfection. He then states the main thesis: character (khuluq) is different from power (qudra). Two reasons: (1) character considers ease of action without prior deliberation; qudra does not. (2) character is not necessarily concurrent with the act, unlike qudra according to Ash'aris. If qudra is equally related to both opposites, character cannot be like that; it must relate to one side. The commentary defends these points, refuting objections from Sharh al-Maqasid and addressing potential counterexamples like character attaching to standing or sitting.

Key Terms
الخلق
القدرة
الملكة
الضدان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Khuluq is the same as qudra or shares its properties.
Correction: Khuluq is distinct because it requires ease of action and is confined to one side of opposites, unlike qudra which is neutral and concurrent.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is true about khuluq?
A) It is a power that can be attributed to both sides equally.
B) It requires ease of action without prior deliberation.
C) It is always concurrent with the act.
D) It is the same as practical wisdom.

Key Takeaway
Khuluq differs from qudra in requiring ease of action and being confined to one opposite, while qudra is neutral and concurrent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on a good deed you perform regularly. Does it come to you with ease or after much struggle and hesitation? According to this lesson, genuine character (khuluq) makes good actions easy—almost automatic. Which virtue in your life is still not 'character' because it requires too much deliberation? What is one concrete step you can take this week to make that action easier until it becomes second nature?
?
The lesson asserts that khuluq must be directed to one side of opposites—you cannot be equally disposed towards good and evil. Think of an area where you have been 'neutral' or inconsistent, perhaps being generous sometimes but stingy other times. Is that a character of generosity or stinginess? Ask yourself: Which side does your heart truly lean towards? What would it mean for you to fully commit to one opposite and train yourself consistently?
?
Consider a bad habit you have, a sin that rolls off your tongue or comes naturally without thinking. That ease is also a kind of 'character'—a corrupt one. Are you comfortable with that? What does this passage teach you about the danger of habits? If khuluq can be good or bad, what is your current character saying about your spiritual state? What one bad habit will you actively work to replace, and how will you make the good opposite easier through repetition and reliance on Allah?

Clarifying Hikma (Wisdom)
Al-Taftazani defines hikma as a faculty for moderate actions and corrects a misconception about its division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct definition of hikma (wisdom) as a faculty producing moderate actions, and how it differs from the division into theoretical and practical wisdom.

Core Idea
Wisdom is a faculty for moderation, not purely theoretical or practical knowledge.

The author begins by rejecting a common division of wisdom into theoretical and practical, clarifying that the true wisdom under discussion is a faculty (malaka) that yields actions between extremes. He then explains why theoretical wisdom is always praiseworthy, unlike practical rationality, and why the composite virtue of justice is inferior to theoretical wisdom. This lesson unpacks these subtle distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to get the overall argument.
2
Identify why the division into theoretical/practical is rejected
The author calls the division a 'false assumption' and gives the correct meaning of wisdom as a faculty. Find the reason for rejection.
3
Understand the definition of wisdom as a faculty
The passage defines wisdom as a faculty from which actions intermediate between instinct and stupidity issue. Note how this differs from knowledge.
4
Note the distinction between practical and theoretical rational faculties
The author says blameworthy excess only applies to the practical faculty, while theoretical is always better when stronger. Understand why.
5
Recognise the superiority of theoretical wisdom over justice
The passage states that justice (composed of wisdom, chastity, courage) is not as perfect as theoretical wisdom, which includes knowledge of Allah and creation.

Definition of Abandonment as a Psychological Quality
Definition of tark with conditions and an alternative view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of tark (abandonment) in Islamic theology, including its conditions and the debate about intention.

Core Idea
Abandonment means not doing what you can do — but only if you could have done it.

In this passage, Sanusi defines the third psychological quality: abandonment (tark). He gives a linguistic definition with important nuances, distinguishing tark from mere non-occurrence. He also presents a second opinion that tark requires intention. Let's explore the details.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style and the presentation of an alternative view.
2
Identify the definition and conditions
The passage defines tark as 'non-performance of the possible'. Note the two conditions that are irrelevant: intention and presence of the opposite action. Also note the key exclusion: what is not possible is not tark.
This distinction is crucial for understanding human responsibility.
3
Consider the debate about intention
The passage presents a second opinion: some say tark requires intention. Ask yourself why this matters for moral and theological attribution of actions.
This debate connects to the broader question of whether abandonment is a voluntary act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثالث : الترك ) بحسب اللغة هو ( عدم فعل المقدور ) سواء كان هناك قصد من التارك أو لا كما في حالة الغفلة ،والنوم، وسواء تعرض لضده أو لم يتعرض، وأما عدم ما لا قدرة عليه، فلا يسمى تركاً ولذلك لا يقال : ترك فلان خلق الأجسام ( وقيل : إن كان قصداً) أي عدم فعل المقدور إنما يسمى ( تركاً إذا كان قصداً )
(Third: Abandonment) According to language, it is (the non-performance of the possible), whether there is an intention from the abandoner or not, as in the case of heedlessness and sleep, and whether it is opposed by its opposite or not. As for the non-occurrence of what one has no power over, it is not called abandonment; therefore it is not said: 'So-and-so abandoned the creation of bodies.' (And it is said: if it is intended?) i.e., non-performance of the possible is only called (abandonment if it is intended).
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage defines the third psychological quality: abandonment (al-tark). It gives the linguistic definition: abandonment is the non-performance of an action that is within one's power (mamdūr). Two conditions are irrelevant: whether the abandoner has an intention or not (e.g., in heedlessness or sleep), and whether the opposite action occurs or not. Critically, the non-occurrence of something one has no power over is not called abandonment; for instance, one does not say 'so-and-so abandoned the creation of bodies' because that is impossible. A second opinion is quoted: some say that non-performance of the possible is only called abandonment if it is intended by the abandoner.

Key Terms
الترك
عدم الفعل
المقدور
القصد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any non-action is considered tark.
Correction: Tark only applies to actions that are within one's power. The non-occurrence of impossible actions is not called abandonment.

Checkpoint
According to the linguistic definition, what is required for something to be called 'tark'?
A) The action must be impossible
B) The action must be within one's power
C) The abandoner must intend not to act
D) The opposite action must occur

Key Takeaway
Tark is the non-performance of what is within one's power, and it need not involve intention according to the linguistic definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What is one act of worship you have been 'abandoning' that you know is within your power? Is your abandonment intentional or due to heedlessness? How does this lesson change how you view that omission, and what will you do about it today?
?
The passage teaches that non-performance of the impossible is not called tark. Are there excuses you use for your shortcomings that are actually within your power to change? Ask yourself honestly: Am I hiding behind 'I cannot' when I truly can?
?
If tark without intention is still tark, what does that mean for your heedless moments? Will you take responsibility for the duties you forget, and what practical step can you take to reduce that heedlessness starting now?

Love as Will (al-Irada)
Definition of love as will (irada)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial definition of love (al-mahabba) as will (al-irada) and how it applies to both divine and human love.

Core Idea
Love is will: God's love is His willing good for us, our love is willing obedience.

This lesson introduces the first definition of love found in our text, where love is identified with will. The author presents this as a straightforward equation: love equals will. We will see how this works for both God and humans.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage for the first definition carefully. Notice the concise style.
The passage is short but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the core equation
The passage equates love with will (al-irāda). See how the author then applies it: God's love is His willing our honor, our love is willing obedience.
3
Reflect on implications
Consider how this definition reframes love as an act of will rather than a passive emotion. How does this affect your understanding of divine love?
This previews the second definition which adds a cognitive dimension.

(المحبة قيل : هي الإرادة فمحبة الله لنا إرادته لكرامتنا) ومثوبتنا على التأييد ) ومحبتنا لله إرادتنا لطاعته وامتثال أوامره ونواهيه، وقد يقال : محبتنا لله سبحانه كيفية روحانية مترتبة على تصور الكمال المطلق الذي فيه على الاستمرار ومقتضية للتوجه التام إلى حضرة القدس بلا فتور وفرار، وأما محبتنا لغيره فكيفية تترتب على تخيل كمال فيه من لذة أو منفعة أو مشاكلة تخيلاً مستمراً كمحبة العاشق لمعشوقه والمنعم عليه والوالد لولده والصديق لصديقه. قوله : ( وقد يقال إلخ ) قسم المحققون من الصوفية المحبة إلى فعلية وصفية وذاتية وفسر الذاتية بميل يكون لمناسبة بين الذاتين من غير اعتبار فعل وصفية وهذا التفسير لا يشملها . قوله : ( الكمال المطلق) أي من كل وجه . قوله على الاستمرار لا بقبوله الصور فإن التصور المستمر على حسب استمراره يوجب المحبة على اختلاف مراتبها . قوله بلا فتور أي بلا فتور في ذلك التوجه والإفراد يعتد بهما . قوله : ( من لذة أي حسية أو منفعة منه يترتب عليه اللذة بعد حصولها أو مشاركة بينهما بوجه والمثالان الأخيران للمشاكلة الأول : باعتبار الجزئية، والثاني : للمشاكلة باعتبار الوصف. قوله : (فمحية الله تعالى إلخ ذكر محبة الله تعالى في عداد الكيفيات النفسانية استطرادي لا لأنها هي التي تختص بذوات الأنفس من الأجسام العنصرية، إذ قد سبق أن الاختصاص المعتبر فيها إضافي بالقياس إلى الذاتيات، بل لأن الصفات القائمة به تعالى ليس من قبيل الأعراض كما سلف .
(Love is said: it is will, so God's love for us is His willing our honor and our eternal reward, and our love for God is our willing His obedience and fulfilling His commands and prohibitions. And it may be said: our love for God, exalted, is a spiritual quality dependent upon conceiving absolute perfection belonging to Him perpetually, and entailing complete orientation toward the holy presence without slackening or fleeing. As for our love for others, it is a quality dependent upon imagining a perfection in them of pleasure, benefit, or similarity, continuously imagined, like the love of a lover for his beloved, the benefactor for the beneficiary, the parent for his child, and the friend for his friend. His statement: 'and it may be said, etc.' The verifiers among the Sufis divided love into actual, attributive, and essential, and interpreted essential as an inclination due to a suitability between two essences without consideration of act or attribute, and this interpretation does not encompass it. His statement: 'absolute perfection' meaning from every aspect. His statement: 'perpetually' not by accepting forms, for continuous conception according to its continuity necessitates love in its varying degrees. His statement: 'without slackening' meaning without slackening in that orientation, and the individual is accounted for both. His statement: 'from pleasure' meaning sensory, or benefit from it entailing pleasure after its attainment, or participation between them in some way, and the last two examples are for similarity: the first according to partiality, the second for similarity according to description. His statement: 'so God's love, etc.' He mentioned God's love among the psychological qualities incidentally, not because it is specific to the souls of elemental bodies, for it has preceded that the specificity considered therein is relative in relation to the essential, but because the attributes subsisting in Him, exalted, are not of the kind of accidents, as previously.)
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage defines love (al-mahabba) as a psychological quality. It begins by stating that love is identified with will (al-irāda). Thus, God's love for us is His willing our honor and eternal reward, while our love for God is our willing obedience to Him. The passage then presents another view: love for God is a spiritual quality resulting from the continuous conception of His absolute perfection, which compels complete orientation toward Him. Love for other beings is similarly a quality resulting from imagining a perfection in them—whether pleasure, benefit, or similarity—and examples are given (lover and beloved, benefactor and beneficiary, parent and child, friend and friend). The following commentary clarifies that the Sufi verifiers classify love into actual, attributive, and essential; the essential is an inclination due to suitability between essences, but this interpretation does not cover all cases. The phrase 'absolute perfection' means perfection from every aspect. 'Perpetually' indicates that continuous conception necessitates love in varying degrees. 'Without slackening' means no weakening in orientation. 'Pleasure' is explained as sensory, or benefit that brings pleasure, or a shared quality. The last two examples illustrate similarity in terms of partiality (parent-child) and description (friend-friend). The final comment notes that God's love is mentioned among psychological qualities incidentally, not because it belongs to soul-essences, but because divine attributes are not like accidents.

Key Terms
المحبة (al-mahabba)
الإرادة (al-irāda)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Love is purely emotional and beyond will.
Correction: The text defines love as will, emphasizing that love involves intention and choice, not just feeling.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is love (al-mahabba) initially defined as?
A) It is a spiritual quality.
B) It is will (al-irāda).
C) It is similarity (al-mushākala).
D) It is an inclination due to suitability.

Key Takeaway
Love is fundamentally an act of will—both divine and human.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If love is truly about what you will, not what you feel, then what does the direction of your will — your actual choices and actions — reveal about your love for God right now?
?
When you obey God, do you do it because you truly will to, or because you feel obligated? How can you transform your obedience into a loving act of will?
?
This passage redefines love as will. If you were to honestly examine your daily intentions, how much of your 'love for God' is real volition versus mere sentiment? What will you intentionally choose to change today?

Love as Spiritual Quality (al-Kayfiyya al-Rūḥāniyya)
Love defined as a spiritual quality based on perception of perfection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second definition of love as a spiritual quality (kayfiyya rūḥāniyya) arising from perception of perfection, and its application to love of God and love of others.

Core Idea
Love for God springs from contemplating His absolute perfection; love for others arises from imagined perfection.

After presenting love as will, the author offers another definition: love is a spiritual quality. This definition explains love as a cognitive response to perceived perfection. We will explore how this applies to love of God and love of creation, including examples and clarifications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the second portion of the source text, starting from 'wa qad yuqalu' to the end. It includes the definition, examples, and commentary.
This is longer; take your time.
2
Distinguish two types of love
Identify the two cases presented: love for God based on conception of His absolute perfection, and love for others based on imagined perfection (pleasure, benefit, similarity).
The examples given illustrate different bases.
3
Examine the commentary
The commentary clarifies terms like 'absolute perfection', 'perpetually', and 'without slackening'. Notice how the Sufi classification is mentioned but critiqued.
Also note the incidental mention of God's love among psychological qualities.

Definition of Abandonment and the Role of Intention
Sanusi defines al-tark and presents the condition of intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic definition of abandonment (al-tark) in Islamic theology, and why intention is a necessary condition for it to be subject to moral evaluation.

Core Idea
Abandonment is defined as not doing what is within power, but only intention makes it blameworthy or praiseworthy.

The concept of abandonment (al-tark) is foundational in kalām for discussions of moral responsibility and divine attributes. The author begins by giving its linguistic meaning and then refines it with a crucial condition: that it be intentional. This lesson unpacks the definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the linguistic definition
The passage defines abandonment as 'not doing what is within power' (ʿadam fiʿl al-maqdūr). Notice that it includes situations with or without intention, like sleep or heedlessness.
The definition is broad at first.
2
Distinguish what is not abandonment
Not doing something that is not within one's power is not called abandonment. For example, one cannot abandon creating bodies.
This highlights the importance of capability.
3
Understand the condition of intention
The passage then presents a view that abandonment is only truly called so when it occurs with intention (qaṣd). This is why a sleeping person is not said to have abandoned writing. Blame and praise attach only to intentional abandonment.
Intention is the key for moral evaluation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثالث : الترك ) بحسب اللغة هو ( عدم فعل المقدور ) سواء كان هناك قصد من التارك أو لا كما في حالة الغفلة ،والنوم، وسواء تعرض لضده أو لم يتعرض، وأما عدم ما لا قدرة عليه، فلا يسمى تركاً ولذلك لا يقال : ترك فلان خلق الأجسام ( وقيل : إن كان قصداً) أي عدم فعل المقدور إنما يسمى ( تركاً إذا كان حاصلاً بالقصد فلا يقال : ترك النائم الكتابة ) ولذلك يتعلق به أي بالترك (الذم) والمدح والثواب والعقاب فلولا أنه اعتبر فيه القصد لم يكن كذلك قطعاً ( وقيل : إنه ) أي الترك ( من أفعال القلوب ) لأنه انصراف القلب عن الفعل وكف النفس عن ارتياده ( وقيل : هو ) أي الترك ( فعل الضد لأنه مقدور والعدم ) أي عدم الفعل ( مستمر) من الأزل ( فلا يصلح أثراً للقدرة الحادثة، وقد يقال : دوام استمراره مقدور لأنه قادر على أن يفعل ذلك الفعل فيزول استمرار ،عدمه فمن هذه الجهة صلح يكون العدم أثراً للقدرة قالوا ولا بد أن يكون كلا الضدين مقدورين حتى يكون ارتكاب أحدهما تركاً للآخر، فإذا لم يكن أحدهما أو كلاهما مقدوراً لم يصح استعمال الترك هناك فلا يقال : ترك بقعوده الصعود إلى السماء، ولا ترك بحركته الاضطرارية حركته الاختيارية ولا ترك بحركته الاضطرارية الصعود. قوله : (حاصلاً بالقصد فيه أن القصد لا يتعلق بالإعدام كما يدل عليه الحديث المرفوع ما شاء الله كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن اللهم إلا أن يراد منه كونه حاصلاً بقصد ما يستلزمه تجوزاً. قوله : ( وكف النفس عن ارتياده أي طلب حصوله ولو كان عدم الفعل المقدور لزم أن يثاب المكلف في أنه مثوبات عدد عدم فعل المنهيات. قوله : ( دوام استمراره أي بقاء استمرار ذلك العدم في الأزمنة الآتية مقدور لا بمعنى أنه يتعلق به القدرة الحادثة حتى يلزم تعلق القدرة بالعدم الأزلي، بل بمعنى أنه يتعلق القدرة بالفعل فيزول استمرار العدم في الأزمنة الآتية بحدوث ذلك الفعل. قوله : ( لا بد أن يكون إلخ) بأن يصلح تعلق القدرة لكل منهما على سبيل البدل .
(The third: abandonment (al-tark). According to language, it is 'not doing what is within power' (ʿadam fiʿl al-maqdūr), whether there is intention from the abandoner or not, as in the state of heedlessness and sleep, and whether he engages its opposite or not. As for not doing what is not within power, it is not called abandonment; therefore it is not said: 'So-and-so left creating bodies.' And it is said: if it is intentional, i.e., not doing what is within power is called abandonment only if it occurs with intention, so it is not said: 'the sleeping person left writing.' Therefore, blame and praise, reward and punishment attach to it (i.e., to abandonment); if intention were not considered in it, it would certainly not be so. And it is said: it (i.e., abandonment) is from the acts of the hearts, because it is the turning away of the heart from the act and the restraining of the soul from seeking it. And it is said: it (i.e., abandonment) is the act of the opposite, because it is within power, while non-existence (i.e., not doing the act) is continuous from eternity, so it is not suitable as an effect of temporal power. And it may be said: the continuation of its persistence is within power, because he is able to do that act, so the persistence of its non-existence ceases; from this aspect, non-existence becomes suitable as an effect of power. They said: it is necessary that both opposites be within power so that committing one is abandonment of the other. If one or both are not within power, the use of abandonment is not valid there. So it is not said: 'he left ascending to the sky by sitting,' nor 'he left his voluntary movement by his involuntary movement,' nor 'he left ascending by his involuntary movement.' His statement: 'occurring with intention'—there is in it that intention does not relate to non-existents, as indicated by the raised hadith: 'What God wills is, and what He does not will is not,' unless it is meant that it occurs with an intention that entails it metaphorically. His statement: 'and restraining the soul from seeking it'—i.e., seeking its occurrence; if not doing the possible act were required, the obligated person would be rewarded for rewards numbering the non-doing of prohibitions. His statement: 'the continuation of its persistence'—i.e., the remaining persistence of that non-existence in future times is within power, not in the sense that temporal power attaches to it such that power attaches to eternal non-existence, but in the sense that power attaches to the act, so the persistence of non-existence in future times ceases by the occurrence of that act. His statement: 'it is necessary that both be, etc.'—i.e., that power can attach to each of them on alternative basis.)
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage defines abandonment (al-tark) as the third psychological quality. It begins by giving the linguistic definition: not doing what is within power, regardless of intention (like heedlessness or sleep) or engagement with its opposite. However, what is not within power is not called abandonment (e.g., not creating bodies). Then it presents several clarified views. The first view: abandonment is only called so if it occurs with intention (bi-l-qaṣd), thus a sleeping person is not said to have left writing. Consequently, blame, praise, reward, and punishment attach to intentional abandonment. The second view: abandonment is an act of the heart, consisting of turning the heart away from the act and restraining the soul from seeking it. The third view: abandonment is the act of the opposite (fiʿl al-ḍidd), because non-existence (ʿadam) of the act is eternal and cannot be an effect of created power. But it may be said that the continuation of non-existence is within power indirectly—one can do the act and thus end the non-existence. Then a condition is stated: both opposites must be within power for one to be called abandonment of the other; e.g., one does not say 'left ascending by sitting' because sitting is not a proper opposite, nor 'left voluntary movement by involuntary movement.' The commentary then explains that intention does not relate to non-existents (as per the hadith), so 'occurring with intention' is metaphorical. 'Restraining the soul' means seeking the act's occurrence; otherwise, leaving prohibitions would incur infinite rewards. The persistence of non-existence is within power only in the sense that power attaches to the act, not directly to the non-existence. Finally, the necessity of both opposites being possible for power is clarified: power can attach to each alternatively.

Key Terms
الترك (al-tark)
عدم فعل المقدور (ʿadam fiʿl al-maqdūr)
القصد (al-qaṣd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is a sleeping person not said to have abandoned writing?
A) Because writing is not within their power.
B) Because they lack intention (qaṣd).
C) Because they are not capable of writing.
D) Because they might be dreaming.

Key Takeaway
Abandonment in kalām is not mere non-doing; it is intentional non-doing of what is within one's power, and only then does it carry moral weight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the past day, have you abandoned a good deed intentionally (perhaps out of laziness) and excused yourself as 'it doesn't count'? How does Sanusi's condition of intention make that excuse invalid?
?
Think of a sin you committed by abandoning an obligation. Did you consider the role of your intention at the moment? How does knowing that only intentional abandonment is blameworthy change your accountability before Allah?
?
What is one act of obedience you have been 'sleepwalking' through—doing without intention? Will you renew your intention today so that your action becomes a true act of worship?

Views on the Nature of Abandonment and the Condition of Opposites
Sanusi discusses views that abandonment is an act of the heart or act of the opposite, and states the condition of opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three views on the nature of abandonment (as an act of the heart or act of the opposite) and the necessary condition that both opposites be within power.

Core Idea
Abandonment is not mere non-existence; it is either an act of the heart or an act of the opposite, and it requires both opposites to be within power.

After defining abandonment and establishing the role of intention, the author presents two further views: that abandonment is an act of the heart (turning away) or an act of the opposite (doing the contrary). A critical condition is then added: for one action to be considered abandonment of another, both must be within one's power. This lesson unpacks these refined understandings.

Guided Path
1
Read the first alternative view
The passage first says abandonment is from the acts of the hearts: it is the turning away of the heart and restraining the soul from seeking the act.
This internalizes abandonment as a spiritual act.
2
Read the second alternative view
The second view states that abandonment is the act of the opposite, because non-existence is eternal and cannot be an effect of temporal power. Instead, one's power directly produces the opposite act, which then constitutes abandonment of the original.
This reframes abandonment as a positive action.
3
Understand the condition of opposites
The passage concludes that for one action to be abandonment of another, both alternatives must be within one's power. Examples illustrate: sitting does not constitute abandoning ascending because ascending is not within power.
This condition links abandonment to capability.

( الثالث : الترك ) بحسب اللغة هو ( عدم فعل المقدور ) سواء كان هناك قصد من التارك أو لا كما في حالة الغفلة ،والنوم، وسواء تعرض لضده أو لم يتعرض، وأما عدم ما لا قدرة عليه، فلا يسمى تركاً ولذلك لا يقال : ترك فلان خلق الأجسام ( وقيل : إن كان قصداً) أي عدم فعل المقدور إنما يسمى ( تركاً إذا كان حاصلاً بالقصد فلا يقال : ترك النائم الكتابة ) ولذلك يتعلق به أي بالترك (الذم) والمدح والثواب والعقاب فلولا أنه اعتبر فيه القصد لم يكن كذلك قطعاً ( وقيل : إنه ) أي الترك ( من أفعال القلوب ) لأنه انصراف القلب عن الفعل وكف النفس عن ارتياده ( وقيل : هو ) أي الترك ( فعل الضد لأنه مقدور والعدم ) أي عدم الفعل ( مستمر) من الأزل ( فلا يصلح أثراً للقدرة الحادثة، وقد يقال : دوام استمراره مقدور لأنه قادر على أن يفعل ذلك الفعل فيزول استمرار ،عدمه فمن هذه الجهة صلح يكون العدم أثراً للقدرة قالوا ولا بد أن يكون كلا الضدين مقدورين حتى يكون ارتكاب أحدهما تركاً للآخر، فإذا لم يكن أحدهما أو كلاهما مقدوراً لم يصح استعمال الترك هناك فلا يقال : ترك بقعوده الصعود إلى السماء، ولا ترك بحركته الاضطرارية حركته الاختيارية ولا ترك بحركته الاضطرارية الصعود. قوله : (حاصلاً بالقصد فيه أن القصد لا يتعلق بالإعدام كما يدل عليه الحديث المرفوع ما شاء الله كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن اللهم إلا أن يراد منه كونه حاصلاً بقصد ما يستلزمه تجوزاً. قوله : ( وكف النفس عن ارتياده أي طلب حصوله ولو كان عدم الفعل المقدور لزم أن يثاب المكلف في أنه مثوبات عدد عدم فعل المنهيات. قوله : ( دوام استمراره أي بقاء استمرار ذلك العدم في الأزمنة الآتية مقدور لا بمعنى أنه يتعلق به القدرة الحادثة حتى يلزم تعلق القدرة بالعدم الأزلي، بل بمعنى أنه يتعلق القدرة بالفعل فيزول استمرار العدم في الأزمنة الآتية بحدوث ذلك الفعل. قوله : ( لا بد أن يكون إلخ) بأن يصلح تعلق القدرة لكل منهما على سبيل البدل .
(The third: abandonment (al-tark). According to language, it is 'not doing what is within power' (ʿadam fiʿl al-maqdūr), whether there is intention from the abandoner or not, as in the state of heedlessness and sleep, and whether he engages its opposite or not. As for not doing what is not within power, it is not called abandonment; therefore it is not said: 'So-and-so left creating bodies.' And it is said: if it is intentional, i.e., not doing what is within power is called abandonment only if it occurs with intention, so it is not said: 'the sleeping person left writing.' Therefore, blame and praise, reward and punishment attach to it (i.e., to abandonment); if intention were not considered in it, it would certainly not be so. And it is said: it (i.e., abandonment) is from the acts of the hearts, because it is the turning away of the heart from the act and the restraining of the soul from seeking it. And it is said: it (i.e., abandonment) is the act of the opposite, because it is within power, while non-existence (i.e., not doing the act) is continuous from eternity, so it is not suitable as an effect of temporal power. And it may be said: the continuation of its persistence is within power, because he is able to do that act, so the persistence of its non-existence ceases; from this aspect, non-existence becomes suitable as an effect of power. They said: it is necessary that both opposites be within power so that committing one is abandonment of the other. If one or both are not within power, the use of abandonment is not valid there. So it is not said: 'he left ascending to the sky by sitting,' nor 'he left his voluntary movement by his involuntary movement,' nor 'he left ascending by his involuntary movement.' His statement: 'occurring with intention'—there is in it that intention does not relate to non-existents, as indicated by the raised hadith: 'What God wills is, and what He does not will is not,' unless it is meant that it occurs with an intention that entails it metaphorically. His statement: 'and restraining the soul from seeking it'—i.e., seeking its occurrence; if not doing the possible act were required, the obligated person would be rewarded for rewards numbering the non-doing of prohibitions. His statement: 'the continuation of its persistence'—i.e., the remaining persistence of that non-existence in future times is within power, not in the sense that temporal power attaches to it such that power attaches to eternal non-existence, but in the sense that power attaches to the act, so the persistence of non-existence in future times ceases by the occurrence of that act. His statement: 'it is necessary that both be, etc.'—i.e., that power can attach to each of them on alternative basis.)
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage defines abandonment (al-tark) as the third psychological quality. It begins by giving the linguistic definition: not doing what is within power, regardless of intention (like heedlessness or sleep) or engagement with its opposite. However, what is not within power is not called abandonment (e.g., not creating bodies). Then it presents several clarified views. The first view: abandonment is only called so if it occurs with intention (bi-l-qaṣd), thus a sleeping person is not said to have left writing. Consequently, blame, praise, reward, and punishment attach to intentional abandonment. The second view: abandonment is an act of the heart, consisting of turning the heart away from the act and restraining the soul from seeking it. The third view: abandonment is the act of the opposite (fiʿl al-ḍidd), because non-existence (ʿadam) of the act is eternal and cannot be an effect of created power. But it may be said that the continuation of non-existence is within power indirectly—one can do the act and thus end the non-existence. Then a condition is stated: both opposites must be within power for one to be called abandonment of the other; e.g., one does not say 'left ascending by sitting' because sitting is not a proper opposite, nor 'left voluntary movement by involuntary movement.' The commentary then explains that intention does not relate to non-existents (as per the hadith), so 'occurring with intention' is metaphorical. 'Restraining the soul' means seeking the act's occurrence; otherwise, leaving prohibitions would incur infinite rewards. The persistence of non-existence is within power only in the sense that power attaches to the act, not directly to the non-existence. Finally, the necessity of both opposites being possible for power is clarified: power can attach to each alternatively.

Key Terms
فعل الضد (fiʿl al-ḍidd)
انصراف القلب (inṣirāf al-qalb)
كف النفس (kaff al-nafs)
القدرة (al-qudra)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required for one action to be considered abandonment of another?
A) The agent must intend both actions.
B) Both actions must be within the agent's power.
C) The agent must have a heart turning away.
D) The action must be voluntary.

Key Takeaway
Abandonment can be understood as an act of the heart or as doing the opposite, and it always requires that both alternatives be within the agent's power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Think of an area where you claim you 'can't' obey Allah—is it a genuine impossibility, or a failure to exert the power He gave you? What is Determination (al-ʿAzm)?
Sharh defines determination (al-ʿazm) as resolve of will after hesitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of determination (al-ʿazm) as the firm resolve of will after hesitation.

Core Idea
Determination is decisive will after inner conflict.

Before we can discuss higher concepts like intention or action, we need a clear understanding of determination. This passage from the Sharh gives us a precise, technical definition. Let's examine what makes determination distinct from mere will or hesitation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its English translation. Notice the key terms: al-ʿazm, jazm al-irāda, al-taraddud, al-dawāʿī.
The Arabic is short but packed with meaning.
2
Identify the four elements
The definition has four parts: (1) what is being defined (al-ʿazm), (2) the genus (jazm al-irāda, firm resolve of will), (3) the differentia (baʿd al-taraddud, after hesitation), and (4) the cause (al-dawāʿī al-mukhtalifa, conflicting motives).
Understanding these parts helps grasp the full concept.
3
Reflect on the hesitation
Hesitation (taraddud) is the state before determination. It arises from conflicting motives pulling in different directions. Determination is the moment the will settles on one option.
This distinguishes determination from random choice or compulsion.
4
Check your understanding
Take the checkpoint quiz to see if you can identify determination from the definition. Then read the commentary for deeper insight.

Source Text

(The fourth of the matters: determination (al-ʿazm), and it is the firm resolve of will after hesitation arising from conflicting motives emanating from those...
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth psychological quality: determination (al-ʿazm). It is described as the firm resolve (jazm) of the will after a state of hesitation (taraddud) that results from different motives (dawāʿī) that arise from various sources. The text cuts off, but the core idea is clear: determination is the decisive moment when the will settles on one course after being pulled in different directions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Determination is just any kind of will or intention.
Correction: Determination (al-ʿazm) specifically refers to firm resolve after hesitation, not mere will or wish.

Checkpoint

B) Firm resolve of will after hesitation.
C) Hesitation itself.
D) The absence of will.

Key Takeaway
Determination (al-ʿazm) is the firm resolve of the will after hesitation caused by conflicting motives.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What would it mean for you to reach a firm resolve of will (al-ʿazm) in this matter? Write down a concrete step you can take today to move towards that determination.

Interpreting Will: Inclination vs. Specifying Attribute
Sanusi argues for interpreting will as inclination, not as a specifying attribute, and addresses an objection about two opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why interpreting will as a specifying attribute leads to a contradiction involving two opposites, and how the author resolves this by interpreting will as inclination or belief in benefit.

Core Idea
The specifying attribute interpretation of will leads to impossibility.

This passage concludes the discussion of psychological qualities by examining the correct interpretation of will. The author argues that if will is understood as a specifying attribute, it leads to a logical contradiction when considering opposites like performing and abandoning an act. Instead, will must be interpreted as inclination or belief in benefit, which avoids the problem.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Note the technical terms: الإرادة (will), العزم (determination), الصفة المخصصة (specifying attribute), الضدين (two opposites).
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two interpretations of will
The author presents two ways to interpret will: (1) as the attribute that specifies one of two possible outcomes, and (2) as inclination or belief in benefit. Find where each is discussed.
The first interpretation is rejected; the second is accepted.
3
Understand the problem with the specifying attribute
The author argues that if will is a specifying attribute, it could specify something not beloved, and determination (عزم) could precede the act. But the specifying attribute must accompany the act, leading to the conjunction of two opposites, which is impossible.
This is the core of the argument.
4
Follow the objection and response
An objector claims that if both opposites (e.g., performing and abandoning prayer) are possible, that contradicts Sharia. The author responds that they are not possible simultaneously, but as alternatives (substitution).
The response preserves the possibility of opposites without logical contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الرابع - خاتمة : في تفسير كيفيات نفسانية من الآراء العقلية والشهوات والنفرات النفسانية، فإن لم يترجح أحد الطرفين حصل التحير وإن ترجح حصل العزم ( وهذا كله أي الذي ذكرناه في تفسير ما عدا الترك ( إنما يصح إذا لم يفسرها ) أي الإرادة ( بالصفة المخصصة لأحد طرفي المقدور بالوقوع ( بل بالميل أو ما يقتضيه من اعتقاد النفع أو ظنه أما إذا فسرناه بالصفة المخصصة فلا يصح لأن الصفة المخصصة قد تخصص ما لا يكون محبوباً ولا مرضياً، والعزم قد يكون سابقاً على الفعل الذي يجب أن تقارنه الصفة المخصصة. بل أن يكون مقارناً للقدرة عند تعلقها به وذلك يفضي إلى اجتماع الضدين وهو محال، وإن كان الثاني فالصلاة غير متروكة لفوات شرط الترك وهو خلاف الشرع واصطلاح العقلاء وأهل اللسان، قلت : ليس مرادهم كون الضدين في الترك مقدورين معاً بل على سبيل البدل وذلك لا ينافي ما ذكر. قوله : ( في تفسير ما عدا الترك وما عدا الرضاء المفسر بترك الاعتراض .
The Fourth Type - Conclusion: On the interpretation of psychological qualities among rational opinions, appetites, and psychological aversions. For if one of the two sides does not preponderate, perplexity occurs; if it preponderates, determination occurs. (All this, i.e., what we have mentioned in the interpretation of what excludes abandonment) is only valid if one does not interpret it (i.e., will) as the attribute that specifies one of the two sides of the possible for occurrence, but rather as inclination or what it entails of belief in benefit or its supposition. But if we interpret it by the specifying attribute, it is not valid, because the specifying attribute may specify what is not beloved nor pleasing, and determination may precede the act which the specifying attribute must accompany. Rather, it must accompany power when it is attached to it, and that leads to the conjunction of two opposites, which is impossible. If the second [interpretation holds], then prayer is not abandoned due to the loss of the condition of abandonment, which is contrary to the Sacred Law, the convention of the rational, and the people of language. I say: They do not mean that the two opposites in abandonment are both possible together, but rather by way of substitution, and that does not contradict what was mentioned. His saying: (On the interpretation of what excludes abandonment and what excludes satisfaction, interpreted as the abandonment of objection).
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is the conclusion of the fourth type of psychological qualities. It begins by stating that previous discussions about interpretation of psychological qualities are valid only if 'will' (al-irāda) is interpreted as inclination or belief in benefit, not as the attribute that specifies one of two possible outcomes. The author explains that if will is taken as a specifying attribute, it could specify something that is neither beloved nor pleasing, and determination (al-ʿazm) could precede the act, but the specifying attribute must accompany the act, leading to the impossibility of two opposites coexisting. An objection is raised: if two opposites (like performing and abandoning prayer) are both possible, this contradicts Sharīʿa and reason. The author answers that the opponents do not mean both are possible simultaneously, but alternately. The passage ends with a marginal comment clarifying the interpretation of 'abandonment' and 'satisfaction'.

العزم
الصفة المخصصة
الضدين
الترك

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Interpreting will as the attribute that specifies one of two possible outcomes is always valid.
Correction: This passage shows that such an interpretation leads to logical problems and is invalid; instead, will should be interpreted as inclination or belief in benefit.

Checkpoint

B) It leads to the conjunction of two opposites
C) It avoids any problems
D) It makes determination unnecessary

Key Takeaway
Interpreting will as an inclination or belief in benefit avoids the logical contradiction that arises from interpreting it as a specifying attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

This lesson distinguishes will as inclination vs. a specifying attribute. Which one truly drives your choices? Could it be that you treated your will as a mere specifying attribute, ignoring the deeper inclination of your heart?
