The Interior Substance Debate: Motion and Space
Analyzing the disagreement on interior substance movement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam debate about whether the interior substance of a moving body is in motion and why it hinges on the definition of space (al-ḥayyiz).

Core Idea
The interior substance debate is really a dispute about what counts as 'space'.

In this passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif, we examine a classic kalam puzzle: when a body moves from place to place, do the inner atoms move as well, or do they stay put? Everyone agrees the outer atoms move, but a disagreement arises over the interior substance. This subtle problem reveals how kalam thinkers argued about motion, space, and composition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two opinions and the reasoning for each. The passage is dense; read slowly.
Pay attention to the terms 'space' (ḥayyiz) and 'interior substance' (jawhar mutawassiṭ).
2
Identify the two opinions
List the two positions: (1) the interior substance moves, (2) it does not move. What arguments support each? The first group uses the impossibility of separation; the second says its space is the surrounding atoms.
3
Trace the core of the disagreement
The passage concludes that the dispute reduces to the interpretation of space. Understand how each group defines 'space' for the interior substance: one as an assumed dimension, the other as the surrounding particles.
This is the key to resolving the issue.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this debate matters for atomistic physics. If the interior substance doesn't move, then motion involves only the outer shell, implying a kind of tearing apart? Yet observation says the body stays intact.
This connects to concepts of continuity and separation (infikāk).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الرابع : الاختلاف في حركة الكون فيما اختلف في كونه متحركاً وذلك في صورتين الأولى إذا تحرك جسم من مكان إلى آخر فاتفقوا على حركة الجواهر الظاهرة منه لأنها قد فارقت أحيازها واختلفوا في الجوهر المتوسط الباطن منه فقيل متحرك وإلا كان ساكناً، إذ لا واسطة بينهما فيما هو قابل لهما بعد أول زمان حدوثه وليس بساكن إذ لو سكن مع حركة باقي الأجزاء لزم الانفكاك وانفصال بعض الأجزاء عن بعض والمحسوس خلافه ولأنه أي الجوهر المتوسط داخل في الكل والكل داخل في حيز الكل فهو داخل في حيز الكل فيكون متحيزاً به أيضاً وقد خرج الجوهر المتوسط عنه أي عن حيز الكل إلى حيز آخر إذ المفروض أن الكل خرج بتمامه عن حيزه، فيكون أيضاً هو متحركاً وقيل الجوهر المتوسط غير متحرك إذ حيزه الجواهر المحيطة به وأنه لم يفارقها ولم ينفصل عنها، فهو مستقر في حيزه، فلا يكون متحركاً والأولون القائلون بكونه متحركاً جعلوه أي جعلوا حيز الجوهر المتوسط هو البعد المفروض الذي يشغله الجوهر المتوسط، وهو بعض من حيز الكل ولا شك أنه قد فارقه فيكون متحركاً، فالاختلاف راجع إلى تفسير الحيز
The fourth observatory - the fourth purpose: the disagreement on the movement of the universe. Concerning what they disagreed about its being moving, and that in two forms: the first, when a body moves from one place to another, they agreed on the movement of its apparent atoms because they have left their spaces, and they disagreed about the interior substance within it. It is said: moving, otherwise it would be stationary, since there is no intermediate between them in what is receptive to both after the first time of its occurrence, and it is not stationary; for if it were stationary with the movement of the remaining parts, separation would follow and the disconnection of some parts from others, and what is perceived is contrary to that. And because it—i.e., the interior substance—is inside the whole, and the whole is inside the space of the whole, so it is inside the space of the whole; thus it is located by it as well. And the interior substance has left it—i.e., the space of the whole—to another space, since it is assumed that the whole has entirely left its space; so it too is moving. And it is said: the interior substance is not moving, since its space is the atoms surrounding it, and it has not left them nor separated from them; so it is settled in its space, so it is not moving. And the first group—those who say it is moving—made its space the assumed dimension which the interior substance occupies, and it is a part of the space of the whole, and there is no doubt that it has left it; so it is moving. Thus the disagreement reduces to the interpretation of space.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage presents the first scenario of disagreement on motion: when a body moves from one place to another. It begins by stating that everyone agrees that the outer atoms (jawāhir ẓāhira) move because they leave their spaces (aḥyāz). But they disagree about the interior substance (al-jawhar al-mutawassiṭ). One opinion says it moves; otherwise it would be stationary, but there is no intermediate state between motion and rest for something receptive to both after the first moment of its existence. If it were stationary while the rest moves, the parts would separate—contrary to observation. Moreover, the interior substance is inside the whole, and the whole is inside its space; so when the whole leaves its space, the interior substance necessarily leaves it too, so it moves. The opposing opinion says it does not move because its space is the surrounding atoms, and it has not left them; it remains settled in its space. The first group defines the interior substance's space as the assumed dimension it occupies, a part of the whole's space, which has been left—hence it moves. The conclusion is that the disagreement is verbal, hinging on the definition of space (al-ḥayyiz).

الحركة
الحيز
الانفكاك

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the disagreement is about a real physical motion versus rest of the interior substance.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the disagreement reduces to the interpretation of 'space' (al-ḥayyiz). The two groups actually agree on the observable phenomena; they differ only in what they mean by the space occupied by the interior substance.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the underlying cause of the disagreement about the movement of the interior substance?
A) A difference in empirical observation
B) A disagreement about the definition of movement
C) A disagreement about the definition of space (al-ḥayyiz)
D) A disagreement about the existence of atoms

Key Takeaway
The debate on the interior substance's motion is resolved by clarifying the definition of space (al-ḥayyiz).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a strong conviction, have you ever examined whether it is based on a clear definition of the key terms involved, or do you assume your understanding is correct without checking? This lesson shows a heated debate can be resolved by asking, 'What do you mean by space?' Where in your life do you need to ask, 'What do you mean by that word?' before continuing an argument?
?
The two groups both had logical arguments, but they disagreed on a fundamental definition. Are you willing to admit that a disagreement you have with someone might stem from a different definition rather than from error on their part? Can you humbly ask them to clarify what they mean before defending your position?
?
The passage warns that if the interior were stationary while the exterior moved, the body would disintegrate—something not observed. How often do you assume your inner state (beliefs, intentions) can remain unchanged while your outer actions change? Are you living with an 'infikāk' between your inner and outer self? Commit today to examining one area where your inner conviction does not match your actions.

The Stationary Person in a Moving Ship: A Verbal Dispute
Sanusi explains the verbal dispute over a stationary person in a ship and three interpretations of space.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the question of whether a stationary person in a moving ship is considered moving depends entirely on the definition of space (al-ḥayyiz).

Core Idea
The dispute over the ship's stationary person is verbal, not real—it hinges on what 'space' means.

In classical kalām, debates about motion often turn on technical definitions. One such case is the stationary person in a moving ship: is he moving or not? This passage reveals that the answer depends on three different definitions of space. The truth is that the dispute is verbal, resolved by clarifying terms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the two opinions
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Identify the two opposing views: one says the person is not moving (like the interior substance), the other says he is moving.
2
Identify the three definitions of space
The passage offers three interpretations of 'space' (al-ḥayyiz): assumed dimension, surrounding atoms, and that which bears weight. Notice how each definition leads to a different conclusion about motion.
3
See how each definition changes the ruling
For each definition, determine whether the stationary person is considered moving. The first definition (assumed dimension) says both the person and interior substance move; the second (surrounding atoms) says the interior substance leaves its space entirely while the person leaves only some; the third (bearer of weight) says the person does not leave his place at all.
4
Understand why the dispute is verbal
The author states clearly that the disagreement is verbal (nazāʿ lafẓī). The correct answer depends on which definition of space one adopts, not on empirical observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك اختلف في المستقر في السفينة المتحركة فقيل ليس بمتحرك كالجوهر المتوسط، فقيل متحرك وكيف لا وأنه أولى بالحركة من الجوهر المتوسط إذ هو يفارق بعض السطح المحيط به أعني الجواهر الهوائية التي أحاطت به من فوقه بخلاف المتوسط فإنه لا يفارق شيئا من السطح المحيط به والحق أنه نزاع لفظي يعود إلى تفسير الحيز كما نبهتك عليه آنفاً فإن فسر بالبعد المفروض كان المستقر في السفينة المتحركة كالجوهر المتوسط لخروج كل منهما حينئذ من حيز إلى حيز آخر وإن فسر بالجواهر المحيطة الوسطاني مفارقاً لحيزه أصلاً وأما المستقر المذكور فإنه يفارق بعضاً من الجواهر المحيطة به دون بعض وإن فسر الحيز بما اعتمد عليه ثقل الجوهر كما هو المتعارف عند الجمهور لم يكن المستقر مفارقاً لمكانه أصلاً . الصورة الثانية قال الأستاذ أبو إسحاق : إذا كان الجوهر مستقراً في مكانه وتحرك عليه جوهر آخر من جهة إلى جهة بحيث تتبدل المحاذاة بينهما فالمستقر في مكانه متحرك وألزم على هذا القول ما إذا تحرك عليه أي على الجوهر المستقر جوهر إلى جهة مخالفة لجهة الآخر فيجب أن يكون الجوهر المستقر متحركاً إلى جهتين مختلفين في حالة واحدة
And likewise they disagreed about the stationary person in the moving ship. It was said: he is not moving, like the interior substance. It was said: moving, and why not? And he is more deserving of motion than the interior substance, since he leaves some of the surrounding surface—I mean the aerial atoms that surrounded him from above—unlike the interior substance, which does not leave anything of the surrounding surface. The truth is that it is a verbal dispute referring back to the interpretation of space, as I reminded you earlier. For if it is interpreted as the assumed dimension, then the stationary person in the moving ship is like the interior substance, each of them at that time moving from one space to another. But if it is interpreted as the surrounding atoms, the interior substance leaves its space entirely, while the mentioned stationary person leaves some of the surrounding atoms but not others. And if space is interpreted as that which bears the weight of the atom, as is customary among the majority, then the stationary person does not leave his place at all. The second scenario: al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq said: If an atom is stationary in its place and another atom moves over it from one direction to another such that the alignment between them changes, then the stationary one in its place is moving. And an objection is made against this statement: if an atom moves over the stationary atom in a direction opposite to the direction of the other, then the stationary atom must be moving in two different directions in a single state.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage covers two related issues. First, the disagreement about the stationary person in a moving ship: one opinion says he is not moving, like the interior substance; the other says he is moving, even more so, because he leaves some surrounding surface (aerial atoms) while the interior substance leaves none. The truth is that this is a verbal dispute depending on the definition of space. Three interpretations are given: (1) space as the assumed dimension—then both the person and the interior substance move; (2) space as the surrounding atoms—the interior substance entirely leaves its space, while the person leaves only some; (3) space as that which bears the weight (as per the common view)—the person does not leave his place at all. Second, the scenario from Abu Ishaq: if a stationary atom has another atom move over it such that their alignment changes, the stationary atom is considered moving. The objection is that if two atoms move over it in opposite directions, it would have to move in two opposite directions simultaneously, which is impossible. This highlights a logical problem with the claim.

Key Terms
السفينة المتحركة
الحيز
النزاع اللفظي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the stationary person in a moving ship is physically moving in the absolute sense.
Correction: The passage shows that whether he is considered moving depends on the definition of space; the dispute is verbal, not factual.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what determines whether the stationary person in a moving ship is considered moving?
A) His actual physical motion
B) The definition of space (al-ḥayyiz)
C) The speed of the ship
D) The number of surrounding atoms

Key Takeaway
The dispute about the moving ship is resolved by clarifying the definition of space, not by empirical observation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Your understanding of core Islamic concepts—like 'iman' or 'tawhid'—is shaped by definitions. Are you certain your definitions are precise, or might they be as ambiguous as the word 'space' in this passage? What will you do to refine them?
?
When you read a kalām argument, do you tend to assume it is about real things or about words? How does that assumption affect your judgment of the disputants? What would it take for you to examine your own assumptions before concluding someone is wrong?

Abu Ishaq's Scenario: The Objection of Opposite Directions
Sanusi presents Abu Ishaq's scenario and the objection from opposite directions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Ishaq's claim that a stationary atom is moving when its alignment with a moving atom changes, and the objection that this leads to contradictory motion in opposite directions.

Core Idea
Abu Ishaq's scenario shows that defining motion by alignment change leads to an absurdity of opposite directions.

A second, more subtle scenario comes from Abu Ishaq. He argues that a stationary atom counts as moving if another atom moves over it and changes their relative alignment. But this definition faces a serious objection: what if two atoms move over it in opposite directions? This would force the stationary atom to move in two opposite directions simultaneously—an impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read Abu Ishaq's claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation for the second scenario. Note: Abu Ishaq says that a stationary atom is moving if another atom moves over it and their alignment changes.
Alignment (muḥādhāh) means the relative positions of the atoms.
2
Understand the objection
The passage then presents an objection: if two atoms move over the stationary atom in opposite directions, then the stationary atom would have to be moving in two opposite directions at the same time. This is absurd.
3
Reflect on the logical consequence
The objection shows that Abu Ishaq's definition of motion (based on alignment change) cannot hold universally because it leads to a contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a change in alignment always indicates motion of the stationary object.
Correction: The objection shows that if two objects move in opposite directions, alignment change would imply contradictory motions, so alignment change alone is not a reliable criterion for motion.

Checkpoint
What is the objection against Abu Ishaq's claim?
A) The moving atom cannot change alignment
B) The stationary atom would have to move in two opposite directions at once if two atoms cross
C) The stationary atom does not actually change alignment
D) The moving atom cannot move in two directions

Key Takeaway
Abu Ishaq's attempt to define motion by alignment change fails because it leads to a logical impossibility when opposite directions are considered.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief or definition that seems straightforward, are you willing to test it with a scenario that forces a contradiction? Abu Ishaq's definition seemed plausible but logically incoherent. Is there a belief you cling to that, when pressed, leads to absurd consequences? Will you examine it today with the same rigor as these kalām scholars?
?
The stationary atom was declared 'moving' because its relation to another atom changed. But the objectors demanded a consistent definition. Are you easily swayed by how others define you or your actions, even when those definitions lead to conflicting demands on your identity? Will you root your self-understanding in a fixed, unchanging truth rather than shifting alignments?
?
The objection required the stationary atom to move in opposite directions simultaneously – an impossibility. Have you ever tried to serve two contradictory masters (e.g., pleasing people and obeying Allah) only to find it impossible? What must you abandon to be consistent in your devotion to the One who is the only true source of direction?

Motion in Two Opposite Directions: Objection and Answer
Presents claim, objection, and answer distinguishing two types of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq's definition of motion leads to an apparent absurdity of moving in two directions, and how the distinction between two types of motion resolves it.

Core Idea
Motion can be from the object or from its place – the objection only applies to one type.

Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq's claim that a stationary substance moves when something moves over it seems to force an absurd consequence: simultaneous motion in two opposite directions. The response introduces a crucial distinction between motion that removes the object from its place and motion that removes its place. This lesson unpacks the argument and shows why the dispute is ultimately terminological.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
Read the opening statement: If a substance is stationary and another moves over it, the stationary one is also moving. This is the claim of al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq.
The key point is that 'motion' here means any change in alignment between two bodies.
2
Grasp the objection
The objection argues that if both substances move toward each other, each moves in a direction opposite to the other, so the stationary one would move in two directions at once – an impossibility.
This objection seems compelling if motion is defined as the object leaving its place.
3
Examine the answer
The answer distinguishes two types of motion: one where the object leaves its place, and one where its place leaves the object. The impossibility only applies to the first type.
In the scenario, the stationary substance undergoes the second type: its place (relative to the moving substance) leaves it, but it does not itself leave its place.
4
See the resolution
The passage concludes that the dispute is merely terminological. Al-Ustadh defines motion broadly as any change in alignment, while opponents restrict it to the first type. Since definitions are conventional, there is no real disagreement.
This shows how careful definition of terms can resolve apparent contradictions in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الصورة ( الثانية ) قال الأستاذ أبو إسحاق : (إذا كان الجوهر مستقراً في مكانه وتحرك عليه ) جوهر (آخر) من جهة إلى جهة ) بحيث تتبدل المحاذاة ) بينهما ( فالمستقر) في مكانه ( متحرك وألزم ) على هذا القول ( ما إذا تحرك عليه ) أي على الجوهر المستقر ( جوهر أنّ كل منهما ( إلى جهة ) مخالفة الجهة الآخر ( فيجب أن يكون ) الجوهر المستقر ( متحركاً إلى جهتين مختلفين ( في حالة واحدة ) وهو باطل بالضرورة ) فيقال : ( لدفع هذا الإلزام : الحركة قسمان قسم يزول به المتحرك عن مكانه، وقسم لا يزول به عنه بل يزول به مكانه عنه ( وذلك ) الذي ذكرتموه من كون الشيء الواحد في حالة واحدة متحركاً إلى جهتين ( إنما يمتنع في حركة يزول بها المتحرك عن مكانه دون ما يزول بها المكان عنه كما في الصورة التي فرضتموها ( وشدد النكير عليه ) أي على قول الأستاذ ) ولا معنى له ) أي للإنكار وتشديده (لأنه نزاع في التسمية) فإنّ الأستاذ أطلق اسم الحركة على اختلاف المحاذيات سواء كان مبدأ الاختلاف في المتحرك أو في غيره فلزمه اجتماع الحركتين إلى جهتين، فالتزمه كما أن جماعة أطلقوا اسم السكون على الكون مطلقاً، فلزمهم تركب الحركة من السكنات بل كون الحركة عن المكان الأول عين السكون في المكان الثاني فالتزموهما، والمخالفون له يطلقونه على القسم الأول ولا مشاحة في الاصطلاحات .
The Second Form: Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq said: (If a substance is stationary in its place, and another substance moves over it from one direction to another such that the alignment [between them] changes, then the stationary [substance] in its place is moving. And it is objected) to this statement (that when a substance moves over the stationary substance, each of them [moves] toward a direction opposite to the direction of the other, so the stationary substance would have to be moving in two different directions in one state), which is necessarily false. So it is said (to repel this objection: Motion is of two types: a type by which the moving object is removed from its place, and a type by which it is not removed from its place but rather its place is removed from it. (And that) which you mentioned—that one thing in a single state is moving in two directions—is only impossible in a motion that removes the moving object from its place, not in that which removes the place from it, as in the form you hypothesized. (And he strongly criticized it), i.e., al-Ustadh's statement, (but it has no meaning), i.e., the denial and its intensification have no meaning, (because it is a dispute about terminology). Indeed, al-Ustadh applied the name 'motion' to the change of alignments, whether the origin of the change lies in the moving object or in something else, so he was compelled to accept the combination of two motions in two directions, and he accepted that. Similarly, a group applied the name 'rest' to existence absolutely, so they were compelled to accept that motion is composed of rests, and that motion from the first place is identical to rest in the second place, and they accepted that. And those who oppose him apply it only to the first type, and there is no quarrel over conventions.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents a second scenario (al-ṣūra al-thāniya) from al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq. He asserts that if a substance A is stationary in its place, and another substance B moves over A from one direction to another (so that B crosses directly over A), then A is actually moving because its alignment with B changes. The objection raised is that if two substances move toward each other (or B moves over A), each moves toward a direction opposite to the other, so A would have to be moving in two opposite directions simultaneously, which is absurd. The answer refutes this by dividing motion into two kinds: (1) motion in which the object moves away from its place, and (2) motion in which the object stays but its place moves away from it. The impossibility of moving in two directions only applies to the first kind. In the given scenario, the stationary substance undergoes the second kind of motion: its place (relative to B) is removed from it, but it does not itself leave its place. The passage also notes that some critics strongly rejected al-Ustadh's view, but this rejection is baseless because the dispute is terminological: al-Ustadh defines motion broadly as any change in alignment, so he accepts the consequence of two opposite motions. Others define motion more narrowly as the object leaving its place. Since it is merely a difference in how the term 'motion' is used, there is no real disagreement.

يزول به المتحرك عن مكانه
يزول به مكانه عنه
نزاع في التسمية
اختلاف المحاذيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is a definitive refutation of al-Ustadh's claim.
Correction: The objection only applies to one definition of motion; al-Ustadh's broader definition avoids the absurdity.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the objection that a stationary substance would have to move in two opposite directions?
A) He denies that the alignment changes.
B) He distinguishes motion that removes the object from its place from motion that removes its place.
C) He admits the impossibility and revises the definition.
D) He argues that two directions are not truly opposite.

Key Takeaway
The apparent absurdity of a stationary substance moving in two directions is resolved by distinguishing motion that moves the object from motion that moves its place, revealing the dispute as merely terminological.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a criticism of your beliefs that seems logically airtight, do you stop to examine whether the critic and you are defining key terms differently? How can this lesson's distinction between two types of motion train you to look for hidden assumptions in arguments?
?
Think of a recent disagreement with someone where both of you were convinced the other was wrong. Could it have been a 'dispute about terminology' like in this lesson? What would it take for you to be humble enough to check if your definitions are causing the conflict?
?
The stationary substance in the lesson is said to 'move' even though it doesn't leave its place. In your spiritual life, are there areas where you are 'moved' by external circumstances even though your core position hasn't changed? How can you distinguish genuine growth from mere change in alignment?

Distinguishing Contiguity, Contact, and Composition
Definitions of contiguity, contact, and composition with their properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three key concepts in atomism—contiguity, contact, and composition—and how they relate to each other according to al-Ash'ari and the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
Contiguity is one and same for an atom, but contact and composition multiply with relata.

Al-Jurjani begins by establishing a critical distinction in Islamic atomism: the difference between mere contiguity (being next to another atom) and the actual contact and composition that entail further implications. This passage lays out the definitions and the positions of al-Ash'ari and the Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read the Arabic passage and translation, focusing on the three key terms: المجاورة, المماسة, التأليف. Note how each is defined in relation to the atom.
2
Identify the positions
Notice that al-Ash'ari and the Mu'tazila argue that contact and composition are distinct from contiguity and are additional attributes that follow from it.
3
Understand the unity of contiguity
The passage states that contiguity is one for an atom even when it touches multiple atoms, while contact and composition are multiple. Think about why this is.
Contiguity is a single state of being adjacent, while each instance of contact is a separate relation.
4
Reflect on the role of separation
Consider how separation (المباينة) is defined as the opposite of contiguity, and how this affects the possibility of composition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الخامس : اتفاق القائلين على وجود جوهر فرد يمكن أن يتخللهما ثالث كما مر ( غير الكون ) الذي يوجب تخصيص الجوهر بحيزة بل هي أمر زائد عليه، وذلك ( لحصوله) أي حصول الكون للجوهر ( حال الانفراد) عما عداه من الجواهر ( دونها ) أي دون المجاورة فإنها غير حاصلة للجوهر حال انفراده عن غيره فيتغايران قطعاً (و) قال الشيخ والمعتزلة أيضاً : ( التأليف والمماسة غير المجاورة بل هما (أمران زائدان على المجاورة ) يتبعان المجاورة ) ويحدثان عقيبها (و) قالوا أيضاً (المباينة أي الافتراق ( المفسر بما تقدم (ضد للمجاورة) التي هي شرط للتأليف فلذلك تنافي المباينة ( التأليف) لأنَّ ضد الشرط ينافي المشروط ( لا لأنه ضده أي لا لأن المباينة بتأويل الافتراق ضد التأليف . (ثم قال الشيخ : ( وحده (المجاورة القائمة بالجوهر الفرد (واحدة) وإن تعدد المجاور له ( وأما المماسة والتأليف فيتعدد كل واحد منهما بحسب تعدد المؤتلف والمماس له ( فهاهنا ) أي فيما إذا أحاط بالجوهر الفرد ستة من الجواهر في جهاته قوله : (بل هما أمران زائدان إلخ يعني أن هناك أمور ثلاثة أحدهما المجاورة والاجتماع وهو من قبيل الكون، وثانيهما مماسة أحدهما للآخر وهي الإضافة المترتبة على الاجتماع وثالثها التأليف وهو كون كل واحد منها بحيث يستلزم حركة أحدها حركة الآخر وهو مترتب على المماسة . قوله : ( عقيبها) عقيباً ذاتياً لا زمانياً. قوله : (ضد للمجاورة ) لكونهما وجوديين يمتنع اجتماعهما لذاتيهما كالحركة والسكون . قوله: (واحدة) لأن المجاورة متماثلة لكونها اجتماعات مخصوصة فلو كانت متعددة لجوهر واحد يلزم اجتماع المثلين بخلاف المماسات والتأليفات فإنها من قبيل الإضافة بتعدد الأطراف، وبخلاف الكون المخصص للجوهر بحيزه حال الانفراد فإنه يخاف الاجتماع لكونه سكوناً فيمكن اجتماعه معه كما قوله : ( التأليف والمماسة غير المجاورة فيه بحث إذ لا دليل على كون المماسة غير المجاورة فإنه لا يمكن تقدير كل منهما دون الآخر فاحتمل أن يكون ذلك الاتحاد المعني قال الأستاذ أبو إسحاق : وبمثل هذا صير إلى أنّ الأمر بالشيء نهى عن أضداده وأن النهي عن الشيء أمر بأحد أضداده. قوله : (ثم قال الشيخ : المجاورة واحدة إلخ ) فيه بحث لأن الجوهر الواحد إذا أحاط به ستة جواهر فهو مجاور لكل واحد منها كما هو مماس له ولا فرق بين المجاورة والمماسة في أن كل منهما ينتفي بعد انتفاء واحد من الستة فالحكم بوحدة المجاورة وتعدد المماسة تحكم .
The Fourth Observatory - The Fifth Objective: Agreement of those who affirm the existence of the indivisible atom. A third can enter between them as mentioned (other than the location) which necessitates specifying the atom to its location, rather it is an additional thing, and that (because its occurrence) i.e., the occurrence of location for the atom (in the state of isolation) from other atoms (without them) i.e., without contiguity, for it is not obtained for the atom in the state of its isolation from others, so they differ absolutely. And al-Shaykh and the Mu'tazila also said: (composition and contact are other than contiguity, rather they are two additional things contingent upon contiguity) and they occur after it. And they also said: (separation, i.e., disunion, interpreted as previously mentioned, is opposite to contiguity) which is a condition for composition, therefore separation contradicts composition because the opposite of the condition contradicts the conditioned, not because it is its opposite, i.e., not because separation by way of disunion is opposite to composition. (Then al-Shaykh said: (contiguity subsisting in the indivisible atom is one) even if the one contiguous to it is multiple, (and as for contact and composition, each of them is multiple according to the multiplicity of what is composed and contacted). (So here), i.e., in the case where six atoms surround the indivisible atom in its directions. Commentary: (His saying: 'rather they are two additional things, etc.') means that there are three things: one is contiguity and conjunction, which is of the category of location; second is the contact of one with the other, which is the relation consequent upon conjunction; third is composition, which is each of them being such that the movement of one necessitates the movement of the other, and it is consequent upon contact. (His saying: 'after it') a subsequent in essence, not in time. (His saying: 'opposite to contiguity') because they are both existents whose conjunction is impossible by their essences, like motion and rest. (His saying: 'one') because contiguity is similar being particular conjunctions, so if it were multiple for one atom, the conjunction of similars would be necessitated, unlike contacts and compositions, for they are of the category of relation with multiple relata, and unlike the location specifying the atom to its location in the state of isolation, for it is not safe from conjunction being because it is rest, so its conjunction with it is possible. (His saying: 'composition and contact are other than contiguity' there is investigation in it, for there is no evidence for contact being other than contiguity, because each cannot be estimated without the other, so it is probable that they are the same in meaning. Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq said: By something like this it is concluded that commanding something is forbidding its opposites, and forbidding something is commanding one of its opposites. (His saying: 'then al-Shaykh said: contiguity is one, etc.') there is investigation in it, because a single atom when six atoms surround it, it is contiguous to each of them just as it is in contact with them, and there is no difference between contiguity and contact in that each of them ceases after the removal of any one of the six, so the judgment of the unity of contiguity and multiplicity of contact is arbitrary.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage defines and discusses three key concepts in atomism: contiguity (mujāwara), contact (mumāssa), and composition (ta'līf). It begins by stating that besides spatial location (kawn), contiguity is an additional attribute that occurs only when atoms are together, not in isolation. Then it presents the view of al-Ash'ari and the Mu'tazila: composition and contact are distinct from contiguity; they are two additional attributes that follow contiguity and occur after it. Separation (mubāyana) is defined as the opposite of contiguity, and since contiguity is a condition for composition, separation contradicts composition. However, the author clarifies that this contradiction is because separation is the opposite of the condition, not because separation is directly opposite to composition. Next, al-Ash'ari's position is given: contiguity subsisting in an atom is one and the same, even if the number of atoms it touches is multiple; but contact and composition are multiple according to the multiplicity of the things composed or contacted. The commentary then expands: there are three distinct things: (1) contiguity/conjunction, akin to location; (2) contact, a relation resulting from conjunction; (3) composition, the mutual implication of movement between atoms, which depends on contact. The commentary also notes that contiguity is one because all instances are similar; if multiple, it would lead to conjunction of similars, unlike relations. A critical gloss questions the distinction: there is no evidence that contact is different from contiguity, as they cannot be conceived separately, so they may be identical. Another gloss critiques the claim that contiguity is one and contact multiple, arguing that if six atoms surround one, that atom is equally contiguous and in contact with each, and both cease if one is removed, making the distinction arbitrary.

Key Terms
المجاورة
المماسة
التأليف
المباينة
الكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that contiguity and contact are the same thing.
Correction: The text argues they are distinct: contiguity is a single attribute of the atom, while contact is a relation that multiplies with the number of atoms contacted.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is contiguity considered one and the same for an atom even when it touches multiple atoms?
A) Because contiguity is a relation that is unaffected by the number of atoms.
B) Because contiguity is a single attribute of the atom that does not multiply, while contact is a relation that does.
C) Because the atom can only be contiguous with one atom at a time.
D) Because contiguity is the same as location.

Key Takeaway
Contiguity is a single attribute of an atom, while contact and composition are multiple relations that depend on contiguity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe that all physical objects are composed of atoms held together by relations. But the lesson teaches that contiguity is not a relation but a single attribute placed by Allah. If Allah were to remove contiguity from an atom, everything would dissolve. How does this truth change the way you view the stability of your own body and the world around you? What will you do today to remind yourself that every connection you see is an act of Allah's constant creation?
?
The lesson distinguishes between contiguity (one) and contact/composition (multiple). This shows that even the simplest physical property reflects Allah's oneness and the multiplicity of creation. When you look at your hand, can you see it as a collection of atoms, each with a single attribute of contiguity, yet forming countless relations? How does this awareness affect your gratitude for the unity and coherence Allah grants to your body every moment? Write down one specific thought you will hold in your mind during your next prayer.
?
Separation (mubaayana) negates composition, meaning that if contiguity is lost, the object disintegrates. You are not a self-sustaining entity; your very physical existence depends on Allah preserving these attributes. Have you ever lived as if your body were permanent and independent? What one habit can you change to acknowledge your utter dependence on Allah for your continued existence?

Critical Examination of the Distinction Between Contiguity and Contact
Critical arguments against the Ash'ari-Mu'tazilite distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critical arguments against the distinction between contiguity and contact, and why some scholars questioned this distinction.

Core Idea
If two concepts cannot be conceived separately, they may be the same thing.

After presenting the Ash'ari-Mu'tazilite position, the text turns to a critical examination. Some scholars argue that there is no evidence to separate contiguity from contact, and that the claim of unity of contiguity and multiplicity of contact is arbitrary. This lesson explores that critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the critical passage
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the phrase 'فيه بحث' and the following arguments.
2
Understand the first critique
The first critique questions whether contact is really distinct from contiguity, because they cannot be imagined without each other.
3
Understand the second critique
The second critique argues that if six atoms surround one, contiguity is still multiple because it ceases with the removal of any one atom, just like contact.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this critique challenges the earlier position and what it says about the nature of these attributes.

المرصد الرابع - المقصد الخامس : اتفاق القائلين على وجود جوهر فرد يمكن أن يتخللهما ثالث كما مر ( غير الكون ) الذي يوجب تخصيص الجوهر بحيزة بل هي أمر زائد عليه، وذلك ( لحصوله) أي حصول الكون للجوهر ( حال الانفراد) عما عداه من الجواهر ( دونها ) أي دون المجاورة فإنها غير حاصلة للجوهر حال انفراده عن غيره فيتغايران قطعاً (و) قال الشيخ والمعتزلة أيضاً : ( التأليف والمماسة غير المجاورة بل هما (أمران زائدان على المجاورة ) يتبعان المجاورة ) ويحدثان عقيبها (و) قالوا أيضاً (المباينة أي الافتراق ( المفسر بما تقدم (ضد للمجاورة) التي هي شرط للتأليف فلذلك تنافي المباينة ( التأليف) لأنَّ ضد الشرط ينافي المشروط ( لا لأنه ضده أي لا لأن المباينة بتأويل الافتراق ضد التأليف . The Fourth Observatory - The Fifth Objective: Agreement of those who affirm the existence of the indivisible atom. A third can enter between them as mentioned (other than the location) which necessitates specifying the atom to its location, rather it is an additional thing, and that (because its occurrence) i.e., the occurrence of location for the atom (in the state of isolation) from other atoms (without them) i.e., without contiguity, for it is not obtained for the atom in the state of its isolation from others, so they differ absolutely. And al-Shaykh and the Mu'tazila also said: (composition and contact are other than contiguity, rather they are two additional things contingent upon contiguity) and they occur after it. And they also said: (separation, i.e., disunion, interpreted as previously mentioned, is opposite to contiguity) which is a condition for composition, therefore separation contradicts composition because the opposite of the condition contradicts the conditioned, not because it is its opposite, i.e., not because separation by way of disunion is opposite to composition. (Then al-Shaykh said: (contiguity subsisting in the indivisible atom is one) even if the one contiguous to it is multiple, (and as for contact and composition, each of them is multiple according to the multiplicity of what is composed and contacted). (So here), i.e., in the case where six atoms surround the indivisible atom in its directions. Commentary: (His saying: 'rather they are two additional things, etc.') means that there are three things: one is contiguity and conjunction, which is of the category of location; second is the contact of one with the other, which is the relation consequent upon conjunction; third is composition, which is each of them being such that the movement of one necessitates the movement of the other, and it is consequent upon contact. (His saying: 'after it') a subsequent in essence, not in time. (His saying: 'opposite to contiguity') because they are both existents whose conjunction is impossible by their essences, like motion and rest. (His saying: 'one') because contiguity is similar being particular conjunctions, so if it were multiple for one atom, the conjunction of similars would be necessitated, unlike contacts and compositions, for they are of the category of relation with multiple relata, and unlike the location specifying the atom to its location in the state of isolation, for it is not safe from conjunction being because it is rest, so its conjunction with it is possible. (His saying: 'composition and contact are other than contiguity' there is investigation in it, for there is no evidence for contact being other than contiguity, because each cannot be estimated without the other, so it is probable that they are the same in meaning. Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq said: By something like this it is concluded that commanding something is forbidding its opposites, and forbidding something is commanding one of its opposites. (His saying: 'then al-Shaykh said: contiguity is one, etc.') there is investigation in it, because a single atom when six atoms surround it, it is contiguous to each of them just as it is in contact with them, and there is no difference between contiguity and contact in that each of them ceases after the removal of any one of the six, so the judgment of the unity of contiguity and multiplicity of contact is arbitrary.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage defines and discusses three key concepts in atomism: contiguity (mujāwara), contact (mumāssa), and composition (ta'līf). It begins by stating that besides spatial location (kawn), contiguity is an additional attribute that occurs only when atoms are together, not in isolation. Then it presents the view of al-Ash'ari and the Mu'tazila: composition and contact are distinct from contiguity; they are two additional attributes that follow contiguity and occur after it. Separation (mubāyana) is defined as the opposite of contiguity, and since contiguity is a condition for composition, separation contradicts composition. However, the author clarifies that this contradiction is because separation is the opposite of the condition, not because separation is directly opposite to composition. Next, al-Ash'ari's position is given: contiguity subsisting in an atom is one and the same, even if the number of atoms it touches is multiple; but contact and composition are multiple according to the multiplicity of the things composed or contacted. The commentary then expands: there are three distinct things: (1) contiguity/conjunction, akin to location; (2) contact, a relation resulting from conjunction; (3) composition, the mutual implication of movement between atoms, which depends on contact. The commentary also notes that contiguity is one because all instances are similar; if multiple, it would lead to conjunction of similars, unlike relations. A critical gloss questions the distinction: there is no evidence that contact is different from contiguity, as they cannot be conceived separately, so they may be identical. Another gloss critiques the claim that contiguity is one and contact multiple, arguing that if six atoms surround one, that atom is equally contiguous and in contact with each, and both cease if one is removed, making the distinction arbitrary.

بحث
دليل
اتحاد
تحكم

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Ash'ari-Mu'tazilite distinction between contiguity and contact is universally accepted.
Correction: This passage shows that there is a critical discussion, with some scholars arguing that they may be the same.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument against the claim that contact is distinct from contiguity?
A) They are both spatial concepts.
B) They cannot be conceived separately, so they may be identical.
C) The Qur'an does not mention them.
D) Al-Ash'ari himself changed his mind.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between contiguity and contact was not universally accepted; critics argued they are inseparable and possibly identical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that a fundamental distinction in kalam may be baseless because the two things cannot be conceived separately. Are there distinctions in your own understanding of Islam or life that you have accepted without evidence? Will you examine them now, or settle for assumptions?
?
The critics argued that al-Ash'ari's ruling on contiguity was arbitrary. How many of your own judgments, in your daily practice of deen or in your interactions, are based on shaky reasoning that you have never questioned? What would it take for you to subject them to the same scrutiny?

Contiguity, Contact, and Composition in Atomist Kalam
The author defines and distinguishes five accidents in atomist ontology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of al-mujawara, al-mumassa, al-ta'lif, and al-mubayana, and how they relate to each other and to al-kawn.

Core Idea
Proximity, contact, and composition are distinct accidents, each with a unique rule.

In this passage, the author clarifies key terms in the atomist framework of kalam. He distinguishes between the accident of location (kawn) and the accident of contiguity (mujawara), then explains that contact and composition are further accidents that depend on contiguity. He also shows how separation opposes contiguity, thus negating composition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical connectors: "غير", "بل", "و".
2
Identify each term and its definition
Identify the definitions of al-kawn, al-mujawara, al-mumassa, al-ta'lif, and al-mubayana as presented in the passage.
Al-mujawara is defined in contrast to al-kawn; al-mumassa and al-ta'lif are defined as additional to al-mujawara.
3
Trace the logical relationships
Understand the causal chain: al-mujawara is a condition for al-ta'lif; al-mumassa follows al-mujawara; al-mubayana is the opposite of al-mujawara and thus negates al-ta'lif.
The passage argues that the opposite of a condition negates the conditioned.
4
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm your grasp of the relationship between al-mujawara and al-mumassa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يمكن أن يتخللهما ثالث كما مر ( غير الكون ) الذي يوجب تخصيص الجوهر بحيزة بل هي أمر زائد عليه، وذلك ( لحصوله) أي حصول الكون للجوهر ( حال الانفراد) عما عداه من الجواهر ( دونها ) أي دون المجاورة فإنها غير حاصلة للجوهر حال انفراده عن غيره فيتغايران قطعاً (و) قال الشيخ والمعتزلة أيضاً : ( التأليف والمماسة غير المجاورة بل هما (أمران زائدان على المجاورة ) يتبعان المجاورة ) ويحدثان عقيبها (و) قالوا أيضاً (المباينة أي الافتراق ( المفسر بما تقدم (ضد للمجاورة) التي هي شرط للتأليف فلذلك تنافي المباينة ( التأليف) لأنَّ ضد الشرط ينافي المشروط ( لا لأنه ضده أي لا لأن المباينة بتأويل الافتراق ضد التأليف.
It is possible that a third (thing) intervenes between them, as previously mentioned (other than al-kawn) which necessitates the specification of the atom to its location, rather it (mujawara) is an additional thing to it. That is because (its occurrence) i.e. the occurrence of al-kawn for the atom (in the state of isolation) from other atoms (without them) i.e. without al-mujawara, for it (mujawara) does not occur for the atom in the state of its isolation from other atoms, so they are necessarily different. And the Shaykh and the Mu'tazila also said: al-ta'lif (composition) and al-mumassa (contact) are not al-mujawara (contiguity), rather they are two additional things to al-mujawara, following al-mujawara and occurring after it. And they also said: al-mubayana (separation) i.e. al-iftiraq (disjunction), interpreted as previously mentioned, is the opposite of al-mujawara, which is the condition for al-ta'lif, therefore al-mubayana negates al-ta'lif, because the opposite of the condition negates the conditioned, not because it (al-mubayana) is the opposite of al-ta'lif itself.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of three accidents related to atoms: al-mujawara (contiguity), al-mumassa (contact), and al-ta'lif (composition). The author first distinguishes al-mujawara from al-kawn (the accident of location): al-kawn specifies an atom to its location when it is isolated, while al-mujawara only occurs when an atom is adjacent to another. They are therefore different. Then, following the Shaykh (i.e. al-Ash'ari) and the Mu'tazila, he asserts that al-ta'lif and al-mumassa are not the same as al-mujawara; they are additional accidents that arise after al-mujawara and depend on it. Additionally, al-mubayana (separation) is the opposite of al-mujawara. Since al-mujawara is a condition for al-ta'lif, the existence of separation negates composition, not because separation is directly opposite to composition, but because it opposes the condition of composition.

التأليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that al-mumassa or al-ta'lif are the same as al-mujawara.
Correction: The text explicitly states that al-mumassa and al-ta'lif are 'two additional things to al-mujawara' and not the same.

Checkpoint

B) Al-mumassa is a type of al-mujawara.

D) Al-mujawara is additional to al-mumassa.

Key Takeaway
In atomist kalam, contiguity, contact, and composition form a causal chain, while separation opposes contiguity and thus prevents composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What specific step will you take today to restore that adjacency with obedience?
?
The author distinguishes between al-kawn (mere existence) and al-mujawara (meaningful proximity). Are there practices in your life where you are merely 'present' (like al-kawn) but not truly 'adjacent' with the intention to form a connection with Allah? How will you move from mere presence to genuine contiguity, then to composition?

Unity of Mujawara and Multiplicity of Mumassa and Ta'lif
Al-Ash'ari distinguishes the unity of mujawara from the multiplicity of mumassa and ta'lif.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-mujawara (contiguity) remains a single accident for a given atom even when it is contiguous to multiple atoms, while al-mumassa (contact) and al-ta'lif (composition) multiply with each distinct relationship.

Core Idea
Mujawara is one; mumassa and ta'lif multiply.

This brief passage from al-Ash'ari draws a sharp distinction between three types of accidental relations among atoms. While one might assume contiguity (mujawara) multiplies like contact and composition, the Shaykh asserts its unity. Understanding this distinction clarifies how relational accidents are counted in kalam ontology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the key contrast between 'one' for mujawara and 'multiplies' for mumassa and ta'lif.
The passage is concise; each word carries weight.
2
Identify the three terms
Find al-mujawara, al-mumassa, and al-ta'lif in the text. Understand that they are all accidental relations of atoms.
3
Reflect on the reasoning
Ask yourself: Why is mujawara one while the others multiply? The commentary suggests mujawara is a homogenous state, while the others are relational (idafa) and vary with the number of terms.
This connects to the broader theory of accidents in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال الشيخ : ( وحده (المجاورة القائمة بالجوهر الفرد (واحدة) وإن تعدد المجاور له ( وأما المماسة والتأليف فيتعدد كل واحد منهما بحسب تعدد المؤتلف والمماس له.
Then the Shaykh said: (And the unity of) al-mujawara subsisting in the indivisible atom is one, even if what is contiguous to it is multiple. As for al-mumassa and al-ta'lif, each of them multiplies according to the multiplicity of that which is composed and that which is in contact.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage presents a further classification by the Shaykh (al-Ash'ari). He asserts that al-mujawara (contiguity) is a single accident for a given atom, even if that atom is contiguous to many other atoms. In contrast, al-mumassa (contact) and al-ta'lif (composition) are multiple: each distinct contact or composition with another atom constitutes a separate instance of these accidents. The reasoning, explained in the surrounding gloss, is that mujawara is a homogenous state (likeness of gatherings), while mumassa and ta'lif are relational (idafa) and vary with the number of terms.

Key Terms
المجاورة (al-mujawara)
المماسة (al-mumassa)
التأليف (al-ta'lif)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume that mujawara multiplies like mumassa and ta'lif, so that a single atom contiguous to four others would have four instances of mujawara.
Correction: Al-Ash'ari explicitly says mujawara is one regardless of multiplicity of contiguous atoms. The multiplicity applies only to mumassa and ta'lif.

Checkpoint

A) Multiple, equal to the number of contiguous atoms.
B) One only.
C) None, because mujawara is not an accident.
D) It depends on the type of atom.

Key Takeaway
Mujawara is a single, homogenous accident, whereas mumassa and ta'lif are relational and multiply with each distinct atom involved.

Six Compositions, Six Contacts, One Mujawara
Enumeration of compositions and contacts in a six-atom surround.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial scenario where an atom is surrounded by six atoms, resulting in six compositions, six contacts, and only one mujawara, and why the six contacts suffice for a seventh kawn.

Core Idea
Six contacts replace a seventh location accident.

Before diving into the Mu'tazila's theory, the text sets up a basic scenario: a central atom surrounded by six others. This configuration involves six compositions, six contacts, but only one 'mujawara' (contiguity). The point is that the six contacts eliminate the need for an additional accident (kawn) to specify the central atom's location. Let's examine this foundational building block.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic carefully, then the English translation. Notice the precise enumerations: six compositions, six contacts, one mujawara.
2
Understand the replacement of kawn
The passage states that the six contacts suffice for a seventh kawn. Ask yourself: why would a seventh kawn be needed? The contacts themselves locate the central atom.
Kawn is an accident that specifies location; here contacts serve that role.
3
Identify the key terms
Make sure you know the meanings of التأليف, المماسة, المجاورة, and الكون in this context.
These are technical kalam terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ست تألیفات) وست مماسات ومجاورة واحدة ( وهي ( أي المماسات الست (تغنيه عن كون سابع يخصصه بحيزه، وقالت المعتزلة : المجاورة بين الجوهر ( الرطب و ) الجوهر ( اليابس تولد (تأليفاً واحداً بينهما (قائماً بهما ) ثم اختلفوا فيما إذا تألف الجوهر مع من الجواهر فقيل : يقوم بالجواهر السبعة تأليف واحد، فإنه لما لم يبعد قيامه بجوهرين لم يبعد قيامه بأكثر ، وإليه أشار بقوله : ( فهاهنا ) أي فيم إذا أحاط بجوهر واحد ستة من الجواهر في جهاته ( تأليف واحد وإذا جاز قيامه بالكثير فلا فرق بين الاثنين وأكثر ، وقيل : ( هاهنا ست تألیفات لاسبع حذراً من انفراد كل جزء ) من الجواهر السبعة ( بتأليف) على حدة ( وأبطلوا ) أي أبطل هؤلاء ( وحدة التأليف) التي ذهب إليها الطائفة الأولى ( بأنه ) قد أن الماهية مضادة لشرط التأليف، أعني المجاورة فتكون منافية له ولا شك أنه ( يزول بمباينة واحدة تأليف جوهر واحد.
Six compositions and six contacts and one mujawara. And they (i.e. the six contacts) suffice for a seventh kawn that would specify it to its location. And the Mu'tazila said: al-mujawara between the moist atom and the dry atom generates a single composition between them subsisting in both. Then they disagreed concerning when the atom composes with some of the atoms. It was said: a single composition subsists in the seven atoms, for since it is not improbable that it subsists in two, it is not improbable that it subsists in more. And to this he pointed by his saying: 'Here' i.e. when a single atom is surrounded by six atoms in its directions, (there is) a single composition. And if it is permissible for it to subsist in many, there is no difference between two and more. And it was said: here there are six compositions, not one, as a precaution against each part of the seven atoms having its own composition. And these (latter) invalidated the unity of composition held by the first group, because the essence (of composition) is opposite to the condition of composition, namely al-mujawara, so it is incompatible with it, and there is no doubt that with a single separation the composition of a single atom ceases.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage discusses the scenario where an atom is surrounded by six atoms. According to the earlier position, there are six compositions, six contacts, but only one mujawara. The six contacts replace the need for a seventh 'kawn' (location accident) that would specify the central atom's location. Then the Mu'tazila are quoted: when a moist atom and a dry atom are contiguous, a single composition is generated between them, subsisting in both. The author then presents a disagreement about composition when multiple atoms combine. One opinion holds that a single composition subsists in all seven atoms (the central plus six surrounding), reasoning that if composition can subsist in two, it can subsist in more. The other opinion holds that there are six separate compositions, one for each pair, to avoid each part having its own composition. The proponents of the second opinion argue against the unity of composition by noting that composition's essence is opposite to its condition (mujawara), so it cannot be one with multiple mujawaras. Furthermore, a single separation destroys the composition of a single atom, implying composition is individual.

Key Terms
التأليف
المماسة
المجاورة
الكون

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Seven
D) Twelve

Key Takeaway
Six contacts remove the need for a separate location accident (kawn).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage enumerates exactly six contacts, six compositions, one mujawara. When it comes to your own understanding of tawhid or other core beliefs, do you settle for vague impressions, or do you strive for the precise, scriptural detail like the scholars do? What would it take for you to replace one vague belief with an exact, well-defined conviction?
?
The six contacts suffice for the seventh kawn—your relationships and actions already define you without needing an extra 'accident' to specify your identity. Where in your life might you be seeking an external label or validation when Allah has already given you sufficient markers through your deeds and connections with others?
?
Mujawara is one overall state, yet it arises from six individual contacts. Think about your community: the many small interactions you have each day form a single spiritual atmosphere. How many of those 'contacts' are intentional acts of faith, and are you aware that they collectively shape your 'mujawara' before Allah?

Mu'tazila View: Composition Generation and Disagreement
Mu'tazila generative composition and subsequent disagreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila claim that a moist and a dry atom generate a single composition, and the subsequent disagreement over whether a group of atoms has one or multiple compositions.

Core Idea
One composition from moist and dry; debate over unity vs. multiplicity.

The Mu'tazila introduce a generative process: when a moist atom and a dry atom are contiguous (mujawara), a single composition is generated between them. But what happens when a central atom is surrounded by six others? Scholars split on whether there is one composition for all or six separate ones. Let's explore this disagreement and the arguments used.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Focus on the Mu'tazila statement and the two opinions that follow. Notice the reasoning for each side.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila generation claim
The Mu'tazila say mujawara between a moist and a dry atom generates a single composition subsisting in both. This is a special case of composition from different types.
Moist and dry are two fundamental qualities of atoms.
3
Analyze the disagreement about multiple atoms
When more atoms are involved, one opinion holds a single composition for all; the other says six separate compositions. Compare their arguments.
The second group argues that composition's essence is opposite to its condition (mujawara), so one composition cannot have multiple mujawaras.
4
Examine the proof against unity
The latter group says that a single separation destroys the composition of a single atom, implying composition is individual to each pair.
This is a key argument: if one separation removes one composition, then compositions are multiple.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ست تألیفات) وست مماسات ومجاورة واحدة ( وهي ( أي المماسات الست (تغنيه عن كون سابع يخصصه بحيزه، وقالت المعتزلة : المجاورة بين الجوهر ( الرطب و ) الجوهر ( اليابس تولد (تأليفاً واحداً بينهما (قائماً بهما ) ثم اختلفوا فيما إذا تألف الجوهر مع من الجواهر فقيل : يقوم بالجواهر السبعة تأليف واحد، فإنه لما لم يبعد قيامه بجوهرين لم يبعد قيامه بأكثر ، وإليه أشار بقوله : ( فهاهنا ) أي فيم إذا أحاط بجوهر واحد ستة من الجواهر في جهاته ( تأليف واحد وإذا جاز قيامه بالكثير فلا فرق بين الاثنين وأكثر ، وقيل : ( هاهنا ست تألیفات لاسبع حذراً من انفراد كل جزء ) من الجواهر السبعة ( بتأليف) على حدة ( وأبطلوا ) أي أبطل هؤلاء ( وحدة التأليف) التي ذهب إليها الطائفة الأولى ( بأنه ) قد أن الماهية مضادة لشرط التأليف، أعني المجاورة فتكون منافية له ولا شك أنه ( يزول بمباينة واحدة تأليف جوهر واحد.
Six compositions and six contacts and one mujawara. And they (i.e. the six contacts) suffice for a seventh kawn that would specify it to its location. And the Mu'tazila said: al-mujawara between the moist atom and the dry atom generates a single composition between them subsisting in both. Then they disagreed concerning when the atom composes with some of the atoms. It was said: a single composition subsists in the seven atoms, for since it is not improbable that it subsists in two, it is not improbable that it subsists in more. And to this he pointed by his saying: 'Here' i.e. when a single atom is surrounded by six atoms in its directions, (there is) a single composition. And if it is permissible for it to subsist in many, there is no difference between two and more. And it was said: here there are six compositions, not one, as a precaution against each part of the seven atoms having its own composition. And these (latter) invalidated the unity of composition held by the first group, because the essence (of composition) is opposite to the condition of composition, namely al-mujawara, so it is incompatible with it, and there is no doubt that with a single separation the composition of a single atom ceases.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage discusses the scenario where an atom is surrounded by six atoms. According to the earlier position, there are six compositions, six contacts, but only one mujawara. The six contacts replace the need for a seventh 'kawn' (location accident) that would specify the central atom's location. Then the Mu'tazila are quoted: when a moist atom and a dry atom are contiguous, a single composition is generated between them, subsisting in both. The author then presents a disagreement about composition when multiple atoms combine. One opinion holds that a single composition subsists in all seven atoms (the central plus six surrounding), reasoning that if composition can subsist in two, it can subsist in more. The other opinion holds that there are six separate compositions, one for each pair, to avoid each part having its own composition. The proponents of the second opinion argue against the unity of composition by noting that composition's essence is opposite to its condition (mujawara), so it cannot be one with multiple mujawaras. Furthermore, a single separation destroys the composition of a single atom, implying composition is individual.

Correction: The second group argues that composition's essence contradicts multiple mujawaras, and a single separation destroys one composition, implying multiplicity.

Al-Ustadh on Mumassa, Mujawara, and Mubayana
Al-Ustadh defines mumassa as identical to mujawara and mubayana as opposite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini's doctrine that mumassa and mujawara are identical and that mubayana is their opposite.

Core Idea
Touching and adjacency are the same accident; separation is their real opposite.

Al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini presents a distinctive view that reduces the number of distinct accidents in atomist physics. He equates two terms—mumassa and mujawara—that others might treat separately, and defines mubayana as their genuine opposite. This passage establishes his position within the broader kalam debate on atomic interactions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how al-Ustadh defines each term relative to the others.
2
Understand the equation
Identify that al-Ustadh equates mumassa with mujawara. He says they are the same reality, though they can be multiplied by different adjacent atoms.
This simplifies the ontology of accidents.
3
Analyze the opposition
Mubayana is said to be opposite to both. Understand the logical chain: mubayana opposes mujawara by consensus, mujawara equals mumassa and ta'lif, so mubayana opposes all.
This shows how definitions cascade.
4
Reflect on significance
Consider why this matters for atomist debates: fewer distinct accidents means a simpler model of physical interaction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الأستاذ: (أبو إسحاق) المماسة بين الجواهر نفس المجاورة بينهما وأنهما متعددان بحسب تعدد المجاور المماس ضرورة، فالمباينة على رأيه ضد لهما حقيقة، وذلك لأنها ضد للمجاورة بالاتفاق والمجاورة عين المماسة والتأليف على أصله فتكون المباينة عنده ضد المماسة والتأليف حقيقة
And al-Ustadh (Abu Ishaq) said: Touching (al-mumassa) between atoms is the same as adjacency (al-mujawara) between them, and they are multiple according to the multiplicity of the adjacent touching [atom], necessarily. So separation (al-mubayana) according to him is an actual opposite to both, because it is opposite to adjacency by agreement, and adjacency is identical to touching and composition (al-ta'lif) according to his principle, so separation according to him is an actual opposite to touching and composition.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage presents the doctrine of al-Ustadh Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini regarding the relationship between touching (mumassa), adjacency (mujawara), and separation (mubayana) among individual atoms (jawahir fard). He asserts that mumassa and mujawara are the same reality, not distinct accidents. They are multiple depending on which atom is adjacent. Mubayana is the direct opposite of both, because it is opposite to mujawara by unanimous agreement, and mujawara is identical to mumassa and also to ta'lif (composition) according to his school. Therefore, mubayana is a genuine opposite to both mumassa and ta'lif. This view reduces the number of distinct accidents by identifying these terms.

Key Terms

مجاورة
مباينة
تأليف
جوهر فرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think mumassa and mujawara are distinct accidents that can occur independently.
Correction: Al-Ustadh explicitly equates them: mumassa is the same as mujawara.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ustadh, what is the relationship between mumassa and mujawara?
A) They are opposites.
B) They are identical.
C) They are distinct but related.
D) They are nonexistent.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ustadh equates mumassa and mujawara, making mubayana their opposite, thereby simplifying the ontology of atomic accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Ustadh collapses touching and adjacency into one accident. In your own life, are there relationships or connections you treat as fundamentally different when they might be the same in essence? How would simplifying your perception of these relationships change how you interact with others?
?
If separation is the opposite of both touching and adjacency, then being apart from something is a genuine metaphysical state. Does your sense of distance from Allah feel like a real and opposite state to closeness? What would it mean to seek mubāyana's opposite—mumāssa and mujāwara—with your Creator?
?
This passage reduces the number of distinct accidents in the world. Are there unnecessary distinctions you maintain in your theology or practice that actually obscure the unified reality of Allah's attributes or actions? What is one distinction you can let go of today for the sake of tawḥīd?

The Unity of Kawn During Contact
Al-Qadi argues the same kawn persists through contact; names vary by consideration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Qadi al-Baqillani's argument that a single kawn (accident of being located) persists unchanged through contact and separation, and that the multiplicity of names (sukun, mujawara, etc.) arises from different considerations.

Core Idea
A single kawn is unchanged; names vary by consideration.

In this passage, Al-Qadi al-Baqillani addresses an important nuance in how an atom's accident of location behaves during contact with other atoms. He argues that the same kawn persists numerically, and that different terms like 'rest' and 'gathering' refer to the same accident under different relations. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and the Ash'ari principles behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Qadi's statement
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how al-Qadi introduces his view: the kawn before and after contact is one.
Focus on the phrase 'واحد لم يتغير ذاته ولم يتعدد'.
2
Understand the scenario
Al-Qadi describes an atom that gains a location, then experiences contact from other atoms, then the contact ceases. Identify the three stages: solitary, contact, separation.
The same kawn is said to be present at all stages.
3
Trace the naming
List the different names given to the kawn in each stage: sukun before contact, ijtima'/mujawara/mumassa during contact, mubayana after. Note that the names differ but the accident is the same.
This is the key insight: names reflect relations, not new entities.
4
Grasp the supporting principles
The commentator endorses al-Qadi's view based on two principles: no need for a specific structure for accidents, and one atom cannot affect another's accident. Understand how these principles rule out alternative views.
Reflect on why this is 'closer to the truth'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال القاضي: (أبو بكر) إذا خص جوهر بحيز أي إذا حصل فيه ثم توارد عليه مماسات ومجاورات من جواهر أخر ثم زالت تلك المماسات والمجاورات عنه فالكون الحاصل لذلك الجوهر قبل وبعد أي قبل المماسات وبعدها واحد لم يتغير ذاته ولم يتعدد وإنما تعددت الأسماء بحسب اعتبارات، فإن الكون الحاصل له قبل انضمام الجواهر إليه يسمى سكوناً والكون المتجدد له حال الانضمام وإن كان مماثلاً للكون الأول يسمى اجتماعاً وتأليفاً ومجاورة ومماسة، والكون المتجدد له بعد زوال الانضمام يسمى مباينة، والأكوان المختلفة على أصله ليست غير الأكوان الموجبة لاختصاص الجوهر بالأحياز المختلفة. وهذا الذي ذكره القاضي أقرب إلى الحق بناء على أصول أصحابنا من عدم اشتراط البينة المخصوصة لقيام عرض من الأعراض بمحله ومن امتناع أن يكون الجوهر أو ما قام به مؤثراً في حكم جوهر آخر، لأن حكم الجوهر يمتنع أن يستفاد مما ليس قائماً به سواء كان مبايناً له أو غير مباين
And al-Qadi (Abu Bakr) said: When an atom is specified with a location, i.e., when it acquires it, then contacts and adjacencies from other atoms come upon it and then those contacts and adjacencies cease, then the kawn (accident of being located) that belongs to that atom before and after—i.e., before the contacts and after them—is one, its essence has not changed and has not become multiple. Rather, the names have become multiple according to considerations. For the kawn that belongs to it before the joining of atoms to it is called 'rest' (sukun), and the kawn that arises for it at the moment of joining, even if it is similar to the first kawn, is called 'gathering' (ijtima'), 'composition' (ta'lif), 'adjacency' (mujawara), and 'touching' (mumassa), and the kawn that arises for it after the cessation of joining is called 'separation' (mubayana). And the different akwan (plural of kawn) according to his principle are none other than the akwan that necessitate the atom's specification with different locations. And this which al-Qadi mentioned is closer to the truth, based on the principles of our companions, due to the non-requirement of a specific structure (al-binya al-makhsusa) for the subsistence of an accident in its locus and due to the impossibility that the atom or what subsists in it should be effective in the ruling of another atom, because the ruling of an atom cannot be derived from what does not subsist in it, whether it is separated from it or not.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage presents the view of al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani on the nature of contact and separation. He argues that the same kawn (accident of occupying a location) remains numerically one throughout the processes of contact and separation. The atom's kawn before any contact is called sukun (rest). When other atoms come into contact, the same kawn is now called ijtima', ta'lif, mujawara, or mumassa—not because a new accident arises, but because the relation changes. After contact ceases, the same kawn is called mubayana. Thus, the multiplicity is only in names, not in the accident itself. The commentator then remarks that this view is closer to the truth based on two Ash'ari principles: first, that no specific structure (like a compound) is required for an accident to subsist in its subject; second, that an atom or its quality cannot be effective in the ruling of another atom. This guards against the idea that contact changes the intrinsic accident of the atom.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that when an atom comes into contact with another, a new accident arises (e.g., a 'contact' accident) distinct from the original kawn.
Correction: Al-Qadi argues that the same kawn persists throughout; the different names reflect relational considerations, not new accidents.

Key Takeaway
