Six Specific Contacts and Unspecified Separations of a Solitary Atom
Explains the six specific contacts and six unspecified separations of a solitary atom, with extensions for additional atoms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a solitary atom can have six specific contacts and six unspecified separations, and how adding atoms reduces the number of unspecified separations.

Core Idea
A solitary atom touches six specific atoms but separates from unspecified ones.

This branch (far') from Ash'ari kalam explores the geometry of atomism. When a single atom exists alone, it can contact six specific atoms (one on each side). But the separations from those contacts are unspecified because the atoms it separates from are not predetermined. This lesson explains the basic scenario and how adding more atoms changes the count.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Focus on the initial statement about the solitary atom and its six specific contacts and six unspecified separations.
Notice the structure: first the solitary case, then extension with added atoms.
2
Understand the concept of six specific contacts
Each contact must be with a specific atom. The solitary atom has six such contacts, presumably in six directions.
In Ash'ari atomism, atoms are assumed to occupy space and can touch adjacent atoms.
3
Grasp the six unspecified separations
Separations are unspecified because the atoms from which it separates are not identified—they could be any atom that is not currently touching.
This is the opposite of the specific contacts.
4
Follow the extension when adding a second atom
When one atom is added, there are now five unspecified separations opposite five specific contacts (since one contact is now the added atom itself).
The pattern continues: adding a third or more atoms reduces the count further.
5
Reflect on the significance
This analysis shows how Ash'ari mutakallimun thought about spatial relationships at the atomic level, which underpins their cosmology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(فروع) على أصول أصحابنا في الاجتماع والافتراق (الأول: الجوهر الفرد المنفرد من غيره يتصور له ست مماسات معينة) لأن ما يماسه لا يكون إلا معينا (وضدها) أي ضد تلك المماسات المعينة (ست مباينات غير معينة لأن ما باينه من الجواهر غير معين فإن ضم إليه جوهر واحد كان فيه خمس مباينات غير معينة مضادة لخمس مماسات معينة وعلى هذا النحو إذا ضم إليه جوهر ثالث أو أكثر) هذا إذا كانت المباينة (قبل المماسة، وأما إذا كانت (بعدها فقال (الشيخ في قول: يضادها) أي يضاد المماسات الست المعينة (ست مباينات غير معينة) كما في القسم الأول (و) قال (في قول: (آخر يضادها (ست) من المباينات (معينة هي (المباينات (الطارئة على المماسات المعينة، قال الآمدي (هذا بناء) من الشيخ ( على أن المماسة وكذا المباينة عرض (غير الكون المخصص
(Branches) based on the principles of our companions concerning joining and separating: (First: For a solitary individual atom isolated from others, six specific touching [contacts] are conceivable because what touches it can only be specific, and their opposite—i.e., the opposite of those specific touches—are six unspecified separations because what separates from it among the atoms is unspecified. For if one atom is joined to it, there are five unspecified separations opposite to five specific touches, and similarly if a third or more atoms are joined.) This if the separation (is before the touching). As for if it (is after it), then al-Shaykh said in one statement: They are opposed by six unspecified separations, as in the first division. And he said in another statement: They are opposed by six specified separations which are the separations that supervene upon the specified touches. Al-Amidi said: This is based (by al-Shaykh) on the view that touching and likewise separation are accidents (other than the specifying kawn).
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a branch (far') derived from the principles of the Ash'ari school regarding joining (ijtima') and separating (iftiraq). It discusses the case of a solitary atom (jawhar fard) isolated from all others. Such an atom can have six specific contacts (mumassat mu'ayyana) because the atom that contacts it must be specific. Conversely, the separation from such contacts is unspecified: the atom could be separated from any of the unspecified surrounding atoms. The passage then distinguishes between separation before contact and after contact. If before contact, the separations are six unspecified. If after contact, there are two views attributed to al-Shaykh (probably Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari or perhaps the Shaykh of the Mu'tazila? The text says 'al-Shaykh'): one view says the six separations are also unspecified, the other says they are specified because they are the separations that arise after the specific contacts. Al-Amidi comments that this discussion is based on the assumption that contact and separation are accidents distinct from the specifying kawn (al-kawn al-mukhassis).

Misconception: One might think that separations are always as specific as contacts.
Correction: The passage shows that separations are initially unspecified because the separated atoms are not determined.

Key Takeaway
A solitary atom can have six specific contacts, but its separations are unspecified until atoms are actually joined to it.

?

Two Views on Separation After Contact
Discusses two views on whether separations after contact are specified or unspecified, and al-Amidi's analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two views of al-Shaykh on whether separations after contact are specified or unspecified, and al-Amidi's comment on the basis of this discussion.

Core Idea
After contact, separations may be specified or unspecified depending on one's premise.

This lesson continues the branch on atomism, focusing on separations that occur after contact (al-mubayana ba'da al-mumassa). Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari reportedly gave two different opinions: one that the separations are still unspecified, and another that they become specified. Al-Amidi explains that this disagreement rests on whether touching and separation are considered accidents distinct from the specifying kawn.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Distinguish between the before-contact and after-contact conditions.
Notice the phrase 'this if the separation is before the touching; as for if it is after it...'
2
Understand the first view
Al-Shaykh's first statement: the six separations after contact are still unspecified, like the before-contact case.
3
Understand the second view
Al-Shaykh's second statement: the six separations after contact become specified, because they arise from the specific contacts.
These are called 'al-mubayanat al-mu'ayyana al-tari'a ala al-mumassat al-mu'ayyana'.
4
Reflect on al-Amidi's analysis
Al-Amidi says this disagreement is based on whether touching and separation are accidents distinct from the specifying kawn (al-kawn al-mukhassis).
This shows how technical assumptions about the nature of accidents affect the analysis.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(فروع) على أصول أصحابنا في الاجتماع والافتراق (الأول: الجوهر الفرد المنفرد من غيره يتصور له ست مماسات معينة) لأن ما يماسه لا يكون إلا معينا (وضدها) أي ضد تلك المماسات المعينة (ست مباينات غير معينة لأن ما باينه من الجواهر غير معين فإن ضم إليه جوهر واحد كان فيه خمس مباينات غير معينة مضادة لخمس مماسات معينة وعلى هذا النحو إذا ضم إليه جوهر ثالث أو أكثر) هذا إذا كانت المباينة (قبل المماسة، وأما إذا كانت (بعدها فقال (الشيخ في قول: يضادها) أي يضاد المماسات الست المعينة (ست مباينات غير معينة) كما في القسم الأول (و) قال (في قول: (آخر يضادها (ست) من المباينات (معينة هي (المباينات (الطارئة على المماسات المعينة، قال الآمدي (هذا بناء) من الشيخ ( على أن المماسة وكذا المباينة عرض (غير الكون المخصص
(Branches) based on the principles of our companions concerning joining and separating: (First: For a solitary individual atom isolated from others, six specific touching [contacts] are conceivable because what touches it can only be specific, and their opposite—i.e., the opposite of those specific touches—are six unspecified separations because what separates from it among the atoms is unspecified. For if one atom is joined to it, there are five unspecified separations opposite to five specific touches, and similarly if a third or more atoms are joined.) This if the separation (is before the touching). As for if it (is after it), then al-Shaykh said in one statement: They are opposed by six unspecified separations, as in the first division. And he said in another statement: They are opposed by six specified separations which are the separations that supervene upon the specified touches. Al-Amidi said: This is based (by al-Shaykh) on the view that touching and likewise separation are accidents (other than the specifying kawn).
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a branch (far') derived from the principles of the Ash'ari school regarding joining (ijtima') and separating (iftiraq). It discusses the case of a solitary atom (jawhar fard) isolated from all others. Such an atom can have six specific contacts (mumassat mu'ayyana) because the atom that contacts it must be specific. Conversely, the separation from such contacts is unspecified: the atom could be separated from any of the unspecified surrounding atoms. The passage then distinguishes between separation before contact and after contact. If before contact, the separations are six unspecified. If after contact, there are two views attributed to al-Shaykh (probably Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari or perhaps the Shaykh of the Mu'tazila? The text says 'al-Shaykh'): one view says the six separations are also unspecified, the other says they are specified because they are the separations that arise after the specific contacts. Al-Amidi comments that this discussion is based on the assumption that contact and separation are accidents distinct from the specifying kawn (al-kawn al-mukhassis).

Key Terms
مباينة بعد المماسة
مماسة
مباينة
عرض
كون مخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that separations after contact are always unspecified like before contact.
Correction: The passage shows that there is a difference of opinion, and some hold they become specified.

Checkpoint
According to al-Amidi, what is the basis for the two views on separation after contact?
A) Whether atoms are divisible
B) Whether touching and separation are accidents distinct from specifying kawn
C) Whether the separations are before or after
D) Number of atoms

Key Takeaway
The specification of separations after contact was disputed among Ash'ari scholars, with al-Amidi linking it to the nature of accidents.

The Isolated Atom: Six Contacts and Six Separations Before Contact
Al-Ījī defines the isolated atom and its six specific contacts and six non-specific separations before contact.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ashʿarī doctrine that an isolated atom has six specific contacts and six non-specific separations when those separations occur before contact.

Core Idea
An atom's contacts are always specific, but its separations before contact are indefinite.

This lesson begins by affirming the agreement on the existence of the indivisible atom (jawhar fard). Al-Ījī then presents the first branch concerning the isolated atom: it is conceivable with six specific contacts. The corresponding separations before contact are non-specific because the atoms from which it separates are not predetermined. This foundational distinction sets the stage for the discussion of combination and separation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the atom's existence and first branch
Read the Arabic text from 'المرصد الرابع' to 'قبل المماسة'. Focus on the definition of the isolated atom and its six contacts.
Notice that the passage begins by stating the agreement and then moves to the branch.
2
Identify the six specific contacts
The text states that the isolated atom has six specific contacts because whatever it contacts must be specific. Reflect on why contacts are necessarily specific.
3
Understand the six non-specific separations before contact
The opposite of the six contacts are six non-specific separations. The text explains that the atoms it separates from are not specified. Consider the example of adding one atom.
This is the key difference: separations before contact are indefinite.
4
Contrast with the case after contact (preview)
The next part of the text will discuss separations after contact, but for this lesson we only focus on the 'before contact' scenario.

المرصد الرابع - المقصد الخامس : اتفاق القائلين على وجود جوهر فرد
قام به مؤثراً في حكم جوهر آخر ، لأن حكم الجوهر يمتنع أن يستفاد مما ليس قائماً به سواء كان مبايناً له أو غير مباين واقتصر المصنف على حكاية هذه المذاهب والتنبيه على أن قول القاضي أقرب إلى الصواب، ولم يتعرض لما أورده الآمدي من تزييفاتها لأنه زيادة تضييع للأوقات . (فروع) على أصول أصحابنا في الاجتماع والافتراق ( الأول : الجوهر الفرد المنفرد من غيره يتصور ) له ست مماسات معينة) لأن ما يماسه لا يكون إلا معينا ( وضدها ) أي ضد تلك المماسات المعينة (ست مباينات غير معينة لأن ما باينه من الجواهر غير معين فإنّ ضم إليه جوهر واحد كان فيه خمس مباينات غير معينة مضادة لخمس مماسات معينة وعلى هذا النحو إذا ضم إليه جوهر ثالث أو أكثر ) هذا إذا كانت المباينة ( قبل المماسة، وأما إذا كانت ( بعدها فقال ) الشيخ ( في قول : يضادها ) أي يضاد المماسات الست المعينة (ست مباينات غير معينة ) كما في القسم الأول ( و ) قال ( في قول : ( آخر يضادها (ست) من المباينات ( معينة هي ( المباينات ( الطارئة على المماسات المعينة، قال الآمدي (هذا بناء) من الشيخ ( على أن المماسة وكذا المباينة عرض (غير الكون المخصص
قوله : ( ومن امتناع إلخ) فلا يمكن أن يكون الجوهر المماس بنفسه أو باعتبار وصف قائم به موجباً بحصول وصف الاجتماع والمماسة والتأليف بجوهر آخر مماس به . قوله : (هذا بناء إلخ) لأنه جعل المماسات الست ضد للمباينات الست، والتضاد إنما يكون في الأمور الموجودة ولو حمل الضد على المنافي، ولو باعتبار يكون الفرع المذكور جارياً على تقدير أن يكون الاختلاف عائداً على التسيمات وإنما زاد الشارح قوله من الشيخ مع أن الآمدي نقل التفصيل المذكور من الأستاذ ثم قال : هذا بناء على أن المماسة والمباينة عرضان غير الكون، فالظاهر أن يكون المعنى من الأستاذ إشارة إلى أن الأستاذ ناقل لهذا التفصيل من الشيخ يدل على ذلك عقد الآمدي الفصل المشتمل على ذلك التفاصيل، بتبعية أحكام الاجتماع والافتراق على أصول أصحابنا لا على الأستاذ وأن كون المماسة والمباينة عرضين غير الكون مذهب الشيخ لا مذهب الأستاذ كما سيجيء من قوله : كما هو مذهب الأستاذ كما نقله
بناء على أن الأصل عدم تعدد الأكوان فما لم تدع ضرورة إلى القول بالتعدد لا يصار إليه، ثم البنية المخصوصة إذا اشترطت وهي تختلف في تلك الأحوال تعددت الأكوان أيضاً ضرورة، وأما إذا لم يشترط لم يلزم التعدد لأن أي عرض قام بمركب جاز قیامه بجوهر فرد . قوله: (ومن امتناع أن يكون الجوهر إلخ ) فلا تبطل الجواهر المتواردة ولا مماستها ومجاورتها حكم الجوهر الأول أعني كونه الأول المسمى سكوناً. قوله : (قال الآمدي : هذا بناء من الشيخ على أن المماسة عرض غير السكون فيه بحث لأن التفصيل المذكور نقله الآمدي في الفصل الثامن الذي عقده لبيان بقية أحكام الاجتماع
للجوهر بحيزه، كما هو بحيز كما هو مذهبه ويرد عليه أنه لم لا يجوز أن يكون ما للجوهر من الكون غير مختلف، ويكون الاختلاف عائداً إلى التسميات، كما ذكره القاضي
Observation 4 - Purpose 5: Agreement of the proponents on the existence of an individual atom. It is established by it as effective in the judgment of another atom, because the judgment of the atom is impossible to be derived from what is not established in it, whether it is separate from it or not separate. The author confined himself to narrating these schools and pointing out that the saying of al-Qāḍī is closer to correctness, and he did not address what al-Āmidī mentioned of their refutations because it is a waste of time. (Branches) on the principles of our companions regarding combination and separation: (The first: the individual atom isolated from others is conceivable) having six specific contacts, because what it contacts can only be specific. (And their opposite) i.e., the opposite of those specific contacts (are six non-specific separations, because what it separates from among the atoms is non-specific. For if one atom is joined to it, it has five non-specific separations opposing five specific contacts, and similarly if a third or more atoms are joined.) This if the separation (is before contact. But if it is after contact, then al-Shaykh said in one opinion: it is opposed by) i.e., it opposes the six specific contacts (six non-specific separations) as in the first case. (And) he said (in another opinion: it is opposed by six specific separations, which are the separations occurring after the specific contacts). Al-Āmidī said: (This is built) by al-Shaykh (on that contact and likewise separation are accidents (other than the specific location). His saying: (And from the impossibility etc.) So it is impossible for the contacting atom by itself or by virtue of a description established in it to be a cause for the occurrence of the description of combination, contact, and composition with another atom contacting it. His saying: (This is built etc.) because he made the six contacts opposites of the six separations, and opposition is only in existing things even if we take the opposite as the contradictory, and even then the mentioned branch proceeds on the assumption that the difference goes back to names. The commentator added his saying 'from al-Shaykh' while al-Āmidī transmitted the mentioned detail from al-Ustādh and then said: This is built on that contact and separation are two accidents other than location. So it appears that the meaning from al-Ustādh is an indication that al-Ustādh is a transmitter of this detail from al-Shaykh, indicating that al-Āmidī's chapter containing these details is based on the rulings of combination and separation according to the principles of our companions, not on al-Ustādh, and that contact and separation being accidents other than location is the doctrine of al-Shaykh, not the doctrine of al-Ustādh, as will come from his saying: as is the doctrine of al-Ustādh as he transmitted. Based on the principle that locations are not multiple unless a necessity calls for multiplicity, then the specific structure when stipulated and varies in those states, the locations also multiply necessarily. But if not stipulated, multiplicity is not necessary because any accident that subsists in a composite may subsist in an individual atom. His saying: (And from the impossibility that the atom etc.) So the substances that are successive and their mutual contact and adjacency do not nullify the judgment of the first atom, namely its being the first called rest. His saying: (Al-Āmidī said: This is built by al-Shaykh on that contact is an accident other than rest. There is discussion because the mentioned detail was transmitted by al-Āmidī in the eighth chapter which he devoted to explaining the remaining rulings of combination. For the atom with its location, as it is with a location as is his doctrine. And it is objected: why may it not be that what belongs to the atom of location is not different, and the difference goes back to names, as al-Qāḍī mentioned.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of the first branch (farʿ) regarding the atom (jawhar fard) according to the principles of the Ashʿarī school. It begins by stating the topic: agreement on the existence of an indivisible atom. The author (al-Ījī) notes that the judgment of an atom cannot be derived from something not inherent in it, and he mentions that al-Qāḍī's view is closest to correct, while al-Āmidī's refutations are not addressed to save time. Then the first branch is introduced: an isolated atom is conceivable with six specific contacts (mumāssāt muʿayyana) because whatever it contacts must be specific. The opposite of these contacts are six non-specific separations (mubāyanāt ghayr muʿayyana) because the atoms it separates from are not specified. If one atom is joined, there are five non-specific separations opposing five specific contacts, and so on. The discussion then addresses whether the separation occurs before or after contact. Before contact, separations are non-specific. After contact, al-Shaykh (al-Ashʿarī) has two opinions: one says the opposite of the six specific contacts is six non-specific separations (same as before contact), and another says the opposite is six specific separations (the separations that arise after the contacts). Al-Āmidī comments that this is built on the view that contact and separation are accidents other than the specific location (kawn mukhassas). The commentary (sharḥ) then explains the text: it discusses the impossibility of the contacting atom being the cause of combination in another atom, and it clarifies that al-Āmidī's attribution of this view to al-Shaykh is based on al-Ustādh (Abū Isḥāq al-Isfarāyīnī) transmitting from al-Shaykh. The sharḥ also notes that the principle of not multiplying locations (akwān) unless necessary, and that if a specific structure is stipulated, locations multiply necessarily. Finally, the passage ends with a response: why can the location not be the same, with difference only in names, as al-Qāḍī held? This sets up the second branch.

Key Terms
جوهر فرد
مماسة
مباينة
كون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the six separations of an atom are always specific, just like its contacts.
Correction: The passage specifies that when separations occur before contact, they are non-specific because the atoms from which it separates are not predetermined.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why are the six contacts of the isolated atom specific?
A) Because the atom is isolated.
B) Because what it contacts can only be specific.
C) Because separations are non-specific.
D) Because of the judgment principle.

Key Takeaway
The isolated atom has six specific contacts, and when separations occur before contact, they are six non-specific separations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson distinguishes between specific contacts (certainties) and non-specific separations (unknowns). In your own pursuit of knowledge about God, are you more anxious about the certainties you can grasp or the uncertainties you cannot? How does this affect your trust in Allah's wisdom?
?
The author chose to skip refutations to avoid wasting time. What is one doubt or debate in your faith that you keep revisiting, even though it distracts you from acting on what you know is true? Will you set it aside today to focus on the clear truths?
?
If the atom's separations are indefinite before contact, what does that imply about your own inability to fully comprehend creation? Are you willing to accept that some aspects of Allah's creation will always remain unknown to you, and how does that humble your heart?

The Six Separations After Contact: Two Opinions of al-Ashʿarī
Al-Ījī presents two opinions of al-Ashʿarī concerning separations after contact, followed by al-Āmidī's comment on the underlying premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two opinions of al-Ashʿarī regarding the six separations after contact: one that they remain non-specific and another that they become specific.

Core Idea
After contact, an atom's separations may be either non-specific or specific, according to two opinions.

The first branch continues with the case of separations after contact. Al-Ashʿarī (al-Shaykh) held two opinions: the first maintains that the six separations are non-specific, just like before contact; the second argues that they become specific because they arise after specific contacts. Al-Āmidī notes that this discussion presupposes that contact and separation are accidents distinct from location.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on separations after contact
Begin from 'وأما إذا كانت بعدها' and read through the two opinions of al-Shaykh. Note the two opinions and the phrase 'قال في قول' indicating a reported view.
2
Differentiate the two opinions
The first opinion says the separations are non-specific like before; the second says they are specific because they arise from specific contacts. Understand the reasoning behind each.
This is a difference in whether separations take on specificity from contacts.
3
Examine al-Āmidī's comment
Al-Āmidī points out that this debate is built on the premise that contact and separation are accidents other than the specific location (kawn mukhassas). This is an important underlying assumption.
The commentary further elaborates on this building.
4
Reflect on the broader context
Consider how this branch relates to the Ashʿarī theory of accidents and the nature of atoms. The discussion about location (kawn) is central.

The commentator added his saying 'from al-Shaykh' while al-Āmidī transmitted the mentioned detail from al-Ustādh and then said: This is built on that contact and separation are two accidents other than location. So it appears that the meaning from al-Ustādh is an indication that al-Ustādh is a transmitter of this detail from al-Shaykh, indicating that al-Āmidī's chapter containing these details is based on the rulings of combination and separation according to the principles of our companions, not on al-Ustādh, and that contact and separation being accidents other than location is the doctrine of al-Shaykh, not the doctrine of al-Ustādh, as will come from his saying: as is the doctrine of al-Ustādh as he transmitted. Based on the principle that locations are not multiple unless a necessity calls for multiplicity, then the specific structure when stipulated and varies in those states, the locations also multiply necessarily. But if not stipulated, multiplicity is not necessary because any accident that subsists in a composite may subsist in an individual atom. His saying: (And from the impossibility that the atom etc.) So the substances that are successive and their mutual contact and adjacency do not nullify the judgment of the first atom, namely its being the first called rest. His saying: (Al-Āmidī said: This is built by al-Shaykh on that contact is an accident other than rest. There is discussion because the mentioned detail was transmitted by al-Āmidī in the eighth chapter which he devoted to explaining the remaining rulings of combination. For the atom with its location, as it is with a location as is his doctrine. And it is objected: why may it not be that what belongs to the atom of location is not different, and the difference goes back to names, as al-Qāḍī mentioned.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a presentation of the first branch (farʿ) regarding the atom (jawhar fard) according to the principles of the Ashʿarī school. It begins by stating the topic: agreement on the existence of an indivisible atom. The author (al-Ījī) notes that the judgment of an atom cannot be derived from something not inherent in it, and he mentions that al-Qāḍī's view is closest to correct, while al-Āmidī's refutations are not addressed to save time. Then the first branch is introduced: an isolated atom is conceivable with six specific contacts (mumāssāt muʿayyana) because whatever it contacts must be specific. The opposite of these contacts are six non-specific separations (mubāyanāt ghayr muʿayyana) because the atoms it separates from are not specified. If one atom is joined, there are five non-specific separations opposing five specific contacts, and so on. The discussion then addresses whether the separation occurs before or after contact. Before contact, separations are non-specific. After contact, al-Shaykh (al-Ashʿarī) has two opinions: one says the opposite of the six specific contacts is six non-specific separations (same as before contact), and another says the opposite is six specific separations (the separations that arise after the contacts). Al-Āmidī comments that this is built on the view that contact and separation are accidents other than the specific location (kawn mukhassas). The commentary (sharḥ) then explains the text: it discusses the impossibility of the contacting atom being the cause of combination in another atom, and it clarifies that al-Āmidī's attribution of this view to al-Shaykh is based on al-Ustādh (Abū Isḥāq al-Isfarāyīnī) transmitting from al-Shaykh. The sharḥ also notes that the principle of not multiplying locations (akwān) unless necessary, and that if a specific structure is stipulated, locations multiply necessarily. Finally, the passage ends with a response: why can the location not be the same, with difference only in names, as al-Qāḍī held? This sets up the second branch.

مماسة
مباينة
كون
عرض
اجتماع
افتراق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might believe that the classification of contacts and separations is purely about the atom itself, not about the metaphysical status of contact and separation as accidents.
Correction: Al-Āmidī explicitly states that the debate is built on the premise that contact and separation are accidents other than the specific location, showing that the classification depends on this underlying assumption.

Checkpoint
According to the second opinion of al-Ashʿarī, what characterizes the six separations after contact?
A) They are non-specific.
B) They are specific.
C) They are both non-specific and specific.
D) They are not defined.

Key Takeaway
After contact, al-Ashʿarī offers two opinions: either the six separations remain non-specific or they become specific, with the debate resting on whether contact and separation are accidents distinct from location.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson reveals that a seemingly simple physical question—what happens after two atoms touch—leads to deep metaphysical assumptions. Think about a belief you hold about the physical world (e.g., the nature of time, space, or causality). What underlying premise is that belief built on, and have you ever examined it? Commit to investigating that premise this week.
?
Al-Āmidī traces the two opinions back to a single premise about the nature of contact and separation. Are there areas in your life where you hold multiple opinions without tracing them back to their foundational assumptions? Challenge yourself to identify one such area and ask: 'What am I assuming here?' Then write down your answer and reflect on whether that assumption is justifiable.

The Intermediate Atom: Proximity and Distance Dispute
Dispute over the intermediate atom's proximity and distance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different views of al-Ashab and al-Ustadh regarding the intermediate atom's proximity and distance, and why the dispute may be merely verbal.

Core Idea
Proximity and distance may be identical in substance but differ in consideration.

This passage delves into a nuanced debate about the intermediate atom. Al-Ashab held that its proximity to one atom is the same as its distance from another, while al-Ustadh argued they are distinct. The discussion reveals deeper assumptions about the nature of location and spatial relations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author presents the problem and the two opposing views.
The passage is dense; take your time to parse the logical flow.
2
Identify the two views
Find where al-Ashab's view is stated and where al-Ustadh's counterexample is given. Understand the reasoning behind each.
Al-Ustadh's proof involves the possibility of the other atom moving along with the intermediate atom.
3
Analyze the verbal resolution
Examine the exception clause that turns the dispute into a verbal one. Understand how interpreting 'proximity is distance' as referring to the same location reconciles the two positions.
This resolution hinges on the concept of 'kawn' (location) and 'i'tibar' (consideration).

The second branch: the atom intermediate between two atoms located in two positions with spaces between them, whenever it approaches one it moves away from the other without doubt. (So the companions said: its proximity is its distance from the other. And al-Ustādh said: otherwise, and it is the truth, for it may approach one without moving away from the other if the other moves with it in the direction of its movement by the amount of its movement. Thus what the companions said is invalidated (except if it is meant that their intention in saying that is that the location is one, i.e., the location described by proximity is the same as the location described by distance) as is the doctrine of al-Ustādh, and there is no additional thing) beyond the location (which are separation and adjacency, so the dispute is verbal), because their intention is that the location itself does not differ, only the considerations differ, and his intention is that the location taken with what it is described by is different. Al-Āmidī said: if a third atom is joined to one of these two atoms, it is without doubt near to the one it is joined to and far from the other. So the companions said: its proximity is the same as its distance from the other. And al-Ustādh said: proximity is not distance. Do you not see that if the distant atom is assumed to join the near one, the intermediate's distance from that distant one is removed, while its proximity to the near one remains. He said: what al-Ustādh mentioned is based on that distance is separation and proximity is adjacency, and that every individual atom has six separations to six atoms, so if it is adjacent to one atom, one separation is removed, leaving five separations according to his principle. And the truth is what the companions mentioned, for it is based on from one of them earlier by his saying: then al-Shaykh and the Muʿtazila and adjacency is combination, i.e., other than location, the principles on individuality without them. His saying: (returning to names) i.e., the considerations to which the names refer. His saying: (al-Āmidī said etc.) means that what the author mentioned contradicts what al-Āmidī mentioned in describing the second branch, for al-Āmidī described it regarding the third atom joined to one of the two atoms, not the intermediate atom, and in the proof transmitted from al-Ustādh he made the location one and denied that separation and adjacency are additional according to al-Ustādh's doctrine. For al-Āmidī transmitted that al-Ustādh holds six additional separations and made the dispute and separation based on the principles of our companions from al-Ustādh Abī Isḥāq. So the commentator's saying: al-Āmidī said: this is built by al-Shaykh, is a place for scrutiny and reflection, for the immediate understanding from the absolute expression is al-Shaykh al-Ashʿarī, unless it is said: this is built on that al-Ustādh transmits it from al-Shaykh even if this transmission is not mentioned in Abkār al-Afkār, or built on the unity of their doctrines, so what is mentioned as based on one of their statements is based on the other's, and what is in it of strain is not hidden. His saying: (if) the joining of the distant atom to the near is assumed etc.) means that the distant one moves to it or it moves to the distant one and the atom joined to it moves with it such that they do not separate.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage presents the second branch (farʿ) concerning the intermediate atom (jawhar mutawassiṭ) located between two atoms. The author states that when this intermediate atom approaches one atom, it necessarily moves away from the other. The companions (al-aṣḥāb, i.e., Ashʿarī scholars) held that its proximity is identical to its distance from the other, meaning they are the same thing. Al-Ustādh (Abū Isḥāq al-Isfarāyīnī) disagreed and said that proximity and distance are different, because it is possible for the atom to approach one without moving away from the other if the other atom moves along with it in the same direction and distance. This, al-Ustādh argues, invalidates the companions' view. However, the author adds an exception: if the companions intended that the location (kawn) itself is one and the same, with only the descriptions (being near or far) differing, then the dispute becomes merely verbal. Al-Āmidī elaborates by considering a third atom joined to one of the two: the intermediate atom is near the one it is joined to and far from the other. The companions say its proximity is the same as its distance, while al-Ustādh says they are different, as can be seen when the distant atom joins the near one—the intermediate's distance to the distant atom disappears but its proximity to the near remains. Al-Āmidī explains that al-Ustādh's view is based on the idea that distance is separation (mubāyana) and proximity is adjacency (mujāwara), and each atom has six separations to six atoms; when adjacent to one, one separation is removed, leaving five. The text then reports that the truth is with the companions. The commentary (sharḥ) further clarifies that the author's description of the second branch differs from al-Āmidī's version, which involves a third atom joined to one of the two, not the intermediate atom itself. The commentator also critically examines al-Āmidī's attribution to al-Shaykh (al-Ashʿarī), suggesting that it might actually refer to al-Ustādh transmitting from al-Shaykh, or that their doctrines are unified, so the attribution is acceptable but somewhat strained.

Misconception: Proximity and distance are always distinct attributes of an atom.
Correction: They can be identical in substance (same location) and differ only in consideration, as per the verbal resolution.

A) The other atom remains stationary.
B) The other atom moves in the opposite direction.
C) The other atom moves with it in the same direction and distance.
D) The intermediate atom itself does not move.

Key Takeaway
The dispute over the intermediate atom's proximity and distance is resolved by recognizing that the location is one and the difference lies only in consideration, making it a verbal disagreement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself in a theological disagreement, do you first ask whether your disagreement is about the same 'location' (haqiqa) or merely different considerations of it? What would change in your conversations if you did?
?
The passage shows that two seemingly opposing statements can be reconciled by recognizing a deeper unity. Is there a conflict in your own life—between your beliefs and your actions, or between you and another person—that might dissolve if you looked for the underlying oneness? Will you take a concrete step to seek that reconciliation today?
?
The companions held a view that al-Ustadh initially rejected, but the author found a way to reinterpret it to align with the truth. How often do you dismiss others' views without trying to find a charitable interpretation? Commit to practicing ta'wil (charitable interpretation) in your next disagreement.

The Intermediate Atom: Are Proximity and Distance One?
Sanusi presents the initial claim and counterexample regarding the intermediate atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the central problem of the intermediate atom and the initial disagreement between the Companions and the Teacher over whether its proximity to one neighbor is identical to its distance from the other.

Core Idea
Proximity and distance may not be two sides of the same coin.

In this branch, we examine a specific case: an atom situated between two others. The natural intuition is that moving closer to one means moving away from the other. But what if the other moves with it? The Companions assert an identity that the Teacher denies. This passage sets up the problem.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the claim of the Companions and the Teacher's counterexample.
2
Identify the two positions
Distinguish the Companions' identity thesis (proximity = distance) and the Teacher's objection (they can be separated by simultaneous movement).
3
Consider the 'unless' clause
The passage hints at a possible verbal resolution if the kawn is unitary. Reflect on how this might change the nature of the dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

على الانفراد دونها. قوله : ( عائداً إلى التسميات) أي الاعتبارات التي ترجع إليها التسميات . قوله : (قال الآمدي إلخ يعني أن ما ذكره المصنف مخالف لما ذكره الآمدي في تصوير الفرع الثاني حيث صور الآمدي في مباينات زائدة وجعل النزاع والافتراق على أصول أصحابنا من الأستاذ أبي إسحاق فقول الشارح: قال الآمدي : هذا بناء من الشيخ، محل نظر وتأمل إذ المتبادر من الإطلاق هو الشيخ الأشعري اللهم إلا أن يقال : هذا بناء على أن الأستاذ ينقله من الشيخ وإن لم يكن هذا النقل مذكوراً في أبكار الأفكار، أو بناء على اتحاد مذهبهما فما ذكر فما هو مبني لكلام أحدهما هو مبني لكلام الآخر، ولا يخفى ما فيه من التعسف . قوله : ذكره القاضي .
The second branch: the atom intermediate between two atoms existing in two loci between which are spaces, whenever it approaches one it becomes distant from the other without doubt. The Companions said: its proximity is its distance from the other. And the Teacher said otherwise, and it is the truth, for it may approach one and not become distant from the other because the other moves with it in the direction of its movement by the amount of its movement; thus what the Companions said is invalidated. Unless it is intended—meaning that their intention in what they said is that the place-event (al-kawn) is one, i.e., the place-event described by proximity is the very same place-event described by distance—as is the doctrine of the Teacher, and there is no additional thing besides the place-event that is separation (al-mubāyanah) and contiguity (al-mujāwarah); thus the dispute is verbal. For their intention is that the place-event itself does not differ; only the considerations differ, and his intention is that the place-event taken along with what it is described by is different. Al-Āmidī said: If a third atom is joined to one of these two atoms, there is no doubt that it is near to the one joined to it and distant from the other. The Companions said: its proximity is identical to its distance from the other. The Teacher said: proximity is not distance. Do you not see that if the distant atom is assumed to join the near one, the intermediate's distance from that distant one is removed, while its proximity to the near one is not removed? He said: What the Teacher mentioned is based on the fact that distance is separation and proximity is contiguity, and that every individual atom has six separations to six atoms; if it becomes contiguous to an atom, one separation is removed and five separations remain, according to his principle. The truth is what the Companions mentioned, for it is based on ... His saying: 'returning to the namings' means the considerations to which the namings refer. His saying: 'al-Āmidī said etc.' means that what the author mentioned contradicts what al-Āmidī mentioned in depicting the second branch, for al-Āmidī depicted it concerning the third atom joined to one of the two atoms, not the intermediate atom, and in the argument transmitted from the Teacher he affirmed the unity of the place-event and denied that separation and contiguity are additional to the Teacher's doctrine. For al-Āmidī transmitted that the Teacher holds six additional separations and made the dispute ... His saying: 'if the joining of the distant atom to the near one is assumed etc.' means that the distant one moves to it or it moves to the distant one, and the atom joined to it moves with it such that they do not separate. And that the place-event subsisting in the atom does not differ; only the namings differ, as al-Qāḍī mentioned.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage discusses the second branch concerning an atom (jawhar) that lies midway between two other atoms, each occupying a distinct location with spaces between them. It first states an accepted principle: whenever this intermediate atom draws nearer to one of its neighbors, it inevitably becomes farther from the other. The Companions (al-ashāb) assert that this very act of drawing near is identical to the act of becoming distant—they are the same reality. The Teacher (al-Ustādh, i.e., Abū Isḥāq al-Isfarāʾīnī) disagrees, and the text declares his view correct: it is possible for the intermediate atom to approach one neighbor without moving away from the other, provided that the other neighbor simultaneously moves toward it by an equal amount. This counterexample refutes the Companions' position. However, a qualification is added: if the Companions intended that the 'place-event' (al-kawn) itself—the attribute of being located—is numerically one, and that the descriptions 'proximity' and 'distance' merely refer to the same single attribute under different considerations, then their view coincides with the Teacher's. In that case, there is no additional entity over and above the place-event called 'separation' (al-mubāyanah) or 'contiguity' (al-mujāwarah). The dispute thus becomes purely verbal. The text then introduces al-Āmidī's further elaboration: when a third atom is joined to one of the two, the intermediate atom is simultaneously near to the joined atom and distant from the other. The Companions again say the proximity is identical to the distance; the Teacher insists they are distinct. The Teacher's argument is that if the distant atom were to join the near one, the intermediate's distance from the distant one would vanish while its proximity to the near one remains—showing they are different. The Teacher's position is based on his ontology: each atom has six distinct separations to six surrounding atoms; contiguity removes only one separation, leaving five. The commentator then notes that the depiction differs between al-Āmidī and the author (al-Ījī), and critiques the attribution to the Shaykh. The passage concludes by reaffirming that the place-event itself does not change; only the names differ, as al-Qāḍī stated.

Key Terms
جوهر فرد (jawhar fard)
كون (kawn)
قرب (qurb)

Correction: The Teacher refutes their literal claim, but the passage later suggests a verbal interpretation where their view aligns with his.

Verbal Dispute: Proximity, Distance, and the Reality of Attributes
Sanusi explains the verbal resolution and al-Āmidī's elaboration on the dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the seemingly real disagreement about proximity and distance can be resolved as a verbal dispute, and the underlying ontology of place-events and separations as presented by al-Āmidī.

Core Idea
The dispute dissolves when we realize 'proximity' and 'distance' are names for the same reality.

Having seen the initial problem, the text now offers a resolution: the disagreement may be merely verbal. If both sides agree that the underlying place-event is one and the same, then the labels 'proximity' and 'distance' refer to the same thing under different considerations. Al-Āmidī's illustration with a third atom deepens the analysis, revealing the ontology of separations and contiguities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the verbal resolution
Focus on the 'unless' clause and al-Āmidī's example. Understand how the dispute becomes verbal if the kawn is unitary.
2
Analyze al-Āmidī's argument
Examine the case of a third atom and the Teacher's argument using separations. Note the conclusion that the truth is with the Companions.
3
Reflect on the ontology
Consider the concept of six separations per atom and how contiguity removes one. Understand why the author sides with the Companions despite the Teacher's reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع ( الثاني : ( الجوهر ( المتوسط بين الجوهرين الكائنين في حيزين بينهما أحياز ) كلما قرب من أحدهما بعد عن الآخر بلا شبهة (فقال الأصحاب : قربه من البعد من الآخر. وقال الأستاذ غيره وهو الحق، إذ قد يقرب من أحدهما ولا يبعد من الآخر بأن يتحرك الآخر معه إلى جهة حركته بمقدار حركته فبطل ما قاله الأصحاب ( اللهم إلا أن يراد أي يكون مرادهم بما قالوه ( أن الكون واحد ) أي الكون الموصوف بالقرب عين الكون الموصوف بالبعد ) كما هو مذهب الأستاذ، وليس ثمة أمر زائد ) على الكون ( هي المباينة والمجاورة فيكون النزاع لفظياً) إذ مرادهم أن نفس الكون لا يختلف إنما المختلف هو الاعتبارات ومراده أن الكون المأخوذ مع ما وصف به ،مختلف قال الآمدي : إذا ضم جوهر ثالث إلى أحد هذين الجوهرين، فلا شك أنه قريب من المنضم إليه وبعيد من الآخر، فقال الأصحاب : قربه عين بعده من الآخر، وقال الأستاذ : القرب غير البعد ألا يرى أنه إذا قدر انضمام الجوهر البعيد إلى القريب زال بعد المتوسط عن ذلك البعيد، ولم يزل قربه من القريب قال : وما ذكره الأستاذ مبني على أن البعد هو المباينة والقرب هو المجاورة، وأن كل جوهر فرد له ست مباينات لستة جواهر فإذا جاور جوهرا فقد زالت مباينة واحدة، وبقيت خمس مباينات على ما هو أصله والحق ما ذكره الأصحاب فإنه مبني من أحدهما سابقاً بقوله : ثم الشيخ والمعتزلة والمجاورة الاجتماع أي غير الكون الأصول على الانفراد دونها. قوله : ( عائداً إلى التسميات) أي الاعتبارات التي ترجع إليها التسميات . قوله : (قال الآمدي إلخ يعني أن ما ذكره المصنف مخالف لما ذكره الآمدي في تصوير الفرع الثاني حيث صور الآمدي في الجوهر الثالث المنضم إلى أحد الجوهرين، لا في الجوهر المتوسط وفي الاستدلال المنقول عن الأستاذ جعل وحدة الكون ونفى كون المباينة والمجاورة زائدة على مذهب الأستاذ، فإن الآمدي نقل أن الأستاذ قائل بست مباينات زائدة وجعل النزاع والافتراق على أصول أصحابنا من الأستاذ أبي إسحاق فقول الشارح: قال الآمدي : هذا بناء من الشيخ، محل نظر وتأمل إذ المتبادر من الإطلاق هو الشيخ الأشعري اللهم إلا أن يقال : هذا بناء على أن الأستاذ ينقله من الشيخ وإن لم يكن هذا النقل مذكوراً في أبكار الأفكار، أو بناء على اتحاد مذهبهما فما ذكر فما هو مبني لكلام أحدهما هو مبني لكلام الآخر، ولا يخفى ما فيه من التعسف . قوله : (إذا) قدر انضمام الجوهر البعيد إلى القريب إلخ ) بأن ينتقل البعيد إليه أو ينتقل هو إلى البعيد وتحرك معه الجوهر المنضم إليه بحيث لم ينفصلا. على أن الكون القائم بالجوهر لا يختلف وإنما يختلف التسميات كما ذكره القاضي .
The second branch: the atom intermediate between two atoms existing in two loci between which are spaces, whenever it approaches one it becomes distant from the other without doubt. The Companions said: its proximity is its distance from the other. And the Teacher said otherwise, and it is the truth, for it may approach one and not become distant from the other because the other moves with it in the direction of its movement by the amount of its movement; thus what the Companions said is invalidated. Unless it is intended—meaning that their intention in what they said is that the place-event (al-kawn) is one, i.e., the place-event described by proximity is the very same place-event described by distance—as is the doctrine of the Teacher, and there is no additional thing besides the place-event that is separation (al-mubāyanah) and contiguity (al-mujāwarah); thus the dispute is verbal. For their intention is that the place-event itself does not differ; only the considerations differ, and his intention is that the place-event taken along with what it is described by is different. Al-Āmidī said: If a third atom is joined to one of these two atoms, there is no doubt that it is near to the one joined to it and distant from the other. The Companions said: its proximity is identical to its distance from the other. The Teacher said: proximity is not distance. Do you not see that if the distant atom is assumed to join the near one, the intermediate's distance from that distant one is removed, while its proximity to the near one is not removed? He said: What the Teacher mentioned is based on the fact that distance is separation and proximity is contiguity, and that every individual atom has six separations to six atoms; if it becomes contiguous to an atom, one separation is removed and five separations remain, according to his principle. The truth is what the Companions mentioned, for it is based on ... His saying: 'returning to the namings' means the considerations to which the namings refer. His saying: 'al-Āmidī said etc.' means that what the author mentioned contradicts what al-Āmidī mentioned in depicting the second branch, for al-Āmidī depicted it concerning the third atom joined to one of the two atoms, not the intermediate atom, and in the argument transmitted from the Teacher he affirmed the unity of the place-event and denied that separation and contiguity are additional to the Teacher's doctrine. For al-Āmidī transmitted that the Teacher holds six additional separations and made the dispute ... His saying: 'if the joining of the distant atom to the near one is assumed etc.' means that the distant one moves to it or it moves to the distant one, and the atom joined to it moves with it such that they do not separate. And that the place-event subsisting in the atom does not differ; only the namings differ, as al-Qāḍī mentioned.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage discusses the second branch concerning an atom (jawhar) that lies midway between two other atoms, each occupying a distinct location with spaces between them. It first states an accepted principle: whenever this intermediate atom draws nearer to one of its neighbors, it inevitably becomes farther from the other. The Companions (al-ashāb) assert that this very act of drawing near is identical to the act of becoming distant—they are the same reality. The Teacher (al-Ustādh, i.e., Abū Isḥāq al-Isfarāʾīnī) disagrees, and the text declares his view correct: it is possible for the intermediate atom to approach one neighbor without moving away from the other, provided that the other neighbor simultaneously moves toward it by an equal amount. This counterexample refutes the Companions' position. However, a qualification is added: if the Companions intended that the 'place-event' (al-kawn) itself—the attribute of being located—is numerically one, and that the descriptions 'proximity' and 'distance' merely refer to the same single attribute under different considerations, then their view coincides with the Teacher's. In that case, there is no additional entity over and above the place-event called 'separation' (al-mubāyanah) or 'contiguity' (al-mujāwarah). The dispute thus becomes purely verbal. The text then introduces al-Āmidī's further elaboration: when a third atom is joined to one of the two, the intermediate atom is simultaneously near to the joined atom and distant from the other. The Companions again say the proximity is identical to the distance; the Teacher insists they are distinct. The Teacher's argument is that if the distant atom were to join the near one, the intermediate's distance from the distant one would vanish while its proximity to the near one remains—showing they are different. The Teacher's position is based on his ontology: each atom has six distinct separations to six surrounding atoms; contiguity removes only one separation, leaving five. The commentator then notes that the depiction differs between al-Āmidī and the author (al-Ījī), and critiques the attribution to the Shaykh. The passage concludes by reaffirming that the place-event itself does not change; only the names differ, as al-Qāḍī stated.

مباينة (mubāyanah)
مجاورة (mujāwarah)
الآمدي (al-Āmidī)
تسميات (tasmīyāt)
كون (kawn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Teacher's view that proximity and distance are distinct is the final truth, and the Companions are entirely wrong.
Correction: The author states that the truth is with the Companions, and the dispute becomes verbal when the kawn is considered unitary.

Checkpoint

A) Two
B) Four
C) Six
D) Eight

Key Takeaway
The dispute over proximity and distance is resolved as verbal when both sides recognize the underlying place-event is one; al-Āmidī's deeper analysis reinforces this with the ontology of separations, yet the author affirms the Companions' position.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you argue about matters of faith or doctrine, are you often defending a particular word or phrase rather than the reality it points to? How might this lesson change the way you approach disagreements with others?

Touching and Separation on Opposite Sides
Determines whether an atom touching another on one side is considered separate on the opposite side.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two positions on whether an atom touching another on one side is considered separate on the opposite side, and why the dispute is terminological.

Core Idea
Touching on one side may or may not imply separation on the other—it depends on your definition.

This lesson explores a subtle question in atomistic kalam: if two atoms are touching on one side, are they separate on the opposite side? Different theologians gave different answers, but the author shows that the disagreement is really about what we mean by 'separation'. Let's examine the problem and its resolution.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
Imagine two atoms touching on one side. The question is: on the other side, are they considered 'separate'? Some said yes because there is no touching there; others said no because touching on one side prevents touching on the other at that moment.
2
Compare the two positions
Identify the view of 'some theologians' (ba'd al-mutakallimin) who affirm separation on the other side, and the view of 'the Teacher' (al-Ustadh) who denies it. Note how each defines separation.
The Teacher is likely Abu Ishaq al-Isfara'ini.
3
Analyze the verbal dispute
The author concludes it's a verbal dispute. Consider: does 'separation' require the possibility of touching? Or the actual absence? That determines which view is correct.
This shows the importance of definitional precision in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع ( الثالث : الجوهر الفرد ( إذا ماس ) جوهراً آخر ( من جهة، فهل يقال : إنه مباين) لذلك الجوهر الآخر ) من الجهة الأخرى ( كما ذهب إليه بعض المتكلمين (لعدم) حصول المماسة في تلك الجهة الأخرى ( أم لا يقال ذلك كما ذهب إليه الأستاذ لأنه لا يمكن المجاورة ) والمماسة ( من تلك الجهة الأخرى ( حينئذ ) أي حين هو مماس له من الجهة الأولى ( وهذا نزاع لفظي ( لأنه إن اعتبر في المباينة إمكان المماسات في تلك الحالة، فالحق هو تلك الحالة، فالحق هو الثاني وإن لم يعتبر فالحق هو الأول.
The third branch: the individual atom, if it touches another atom on one side, is it said that it is separate from that other atom on the other side, as some theologians hold due to the absence of touching on that other side? Or is it not said, as the Teacher holds, because contiguity and touching from that other side are impossible at that time, i.e., when it is touching it from the first side? And this is a verbal dispute, because if one considers in separation the possibility of touchings in that state, the truth is the second [position]; and if one does not consider it, the truth is the first.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage discusses the third branch concerning a single atom (jawhar fard) that makes contact (māssa) with another atom on one side. The question is whether it can be described as 'separate' (mubāyin) from that other atom on the opposite side. Some theologians (baʿḍ al-mutakallimīn) affirm this, arguing that since no touching occurs on the other side, separation exists there. The Teacher (al-Ustādh) denies it, because at the moment of contact on the first side, it is impossible for contiguity (mujāwarah) or touching (mumāssah) to occur on the other side simultaneously with contact on the first. The text concludes that this is a verbal dispute: if one defines separation by the mere possibility of contact in that state, then the Teacher's view is correct; if one requires actual contact, then the first view is correct. The resolution depends on the criterion for applying the term 'separation'.

Key Terms
مماسة (mumāssah, touching)
مباينة (mubāyanah, separation)
مجاورة (mujāwarah, contiguity)
جهة (jiha, side)
إمكان (imkān, possibility)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The dispute is about a real physical difference between atoms.
Correction: The author clarifies it is a verbal dispute about the definition of 'separation'.

Checkpoint
Under which condition does the Teacher (al-Ustādh) deny that an atom is described as separate on the side opposite to its touching?
A) Because touching on the first side makes touching on the other side impossible at that moment
B) Because the atom has no other side
C) Because separation requires distance in space
D) Because the atom is indivisible

Key Takeaway
Whether an atom is considered separate on the side opposite to its touching depends on whether separation is defined by the impossibility of touching or by the actual absence of touching.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Think of a disagreement you have had with someone about faith or life. Could it be that you were both using the same word (like 'justice', 'mercy', or 'guidance') with different definitions? How might recognizing a 'verbal dispute' change the way you approach that disagreement today?
?
This lesson shows that even a simple physical question about atoms can reduce to a matter of definition. In your own journey of learning Islam, are there areas where you hold strong opinions that might actually rest more on terminology than on substance? How can you test that?
?
The Teacher (al-Ustādh) chose a definition that made his position correct. Are there areas in your life where you choose definitions that make your view correct, while avoiding a deeper truth? What would it take for you to prioritize reality over terminology?

Universal Separation of Atoms: The Fourth Branch
Debate on universal separation of atoms with objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether all atoms in the world can exist in a state of universal separation, and why the possibility of contiguity suffices for separation.

Core Idea
Separation only requires the possibility of contiguity, not actual simultaneous contact.

This lesson examines a specific branch of kalam atomism: can all atoms be completely separated from one another? One school denies this because universal contiguity is impossible, but the author argues that separation only needs potential contiguity between any two atoms, not simultaneous contact among all. We will see how this resolves the objection and note a textual nuance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage along with its literal translation. Pay attention to the structure: a statement of the branch, two opposing views, and the author's response followed by a textual note.
The passage is dense, so read it slowly.
2
Identify the two positions
Notice that one view permits universal separation, while the other prohibits it due to the impossibility of contiguity among all atoms simultaneously. Understand the reasoning behind each.
3
Examine the objection
The second view argues that separation requires the possibility of contiguity, but since contiguity among all atoms is impossible, separation cannot be described. This is a key logical point.
The objection is based on the condition of contiguity.
4
Understand the author's response
Al-Ījī responds that for separation to be valid, it suffices that contiguity is permissible 'alternatively' (badalan) – i.e., between any given pair, not all at once. This dissolves the objection.
The response hinges on the concept of potential contact.
5
Note the textual correction
The commentator points out a likely scribal error: the phrase 'as if they were altered' should probably read 'if they were faced away,' as alteration is irrelevant to disjunction. Reflect on how textual precision matters in theology.
This shows the importance of textual criticism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرع ( الرابع : يجوز المباينة والافتراق في جملة جواهر (العالم بحيث لا يتصف شيء منها بالاجتماع مع غيره كما إذا تبدلت وزال تركيبها بالكلية ( وقيل: لا ) يجوز (إذ لا تجوز المجاورة بين الكل ولا بد في المباينة من إمكان المجاورة قال المصنف : ( ويكفي) يعني في الوصف بالمباينة (جوازها) أي جواز المجاورة بين الكل (بدلاً ) ولا شبهة في هذا الجواز، وإنما الممتنع هو المجاورة بين الكل على الاجتماع. قوله : (كما إذا تبدلت الصواب إذا استدبرت إذ لا مدخل للتبديل في حصول الافتراق ولعله تصحيف من الكتاب.
The fourth branch: It is permissible that separation and disjunction occur among all the atoms of the world, such that none of them is characterized by conjunction with another, as if they were altered and their composition was completely removed. And it was said: it is not permissible, because contiguity among all is not possible, and separation requires the possibility of contiguity. The author said: 'And it suffices'—meaning in describing separation—'its permissibility,' i.e., the permissibility of contiguity among all, 'alternatively.' And there is no doubt about this permissibility; what is impossible is contiguity among all simultaneously. His saying: 'as if they were altered' —the correct [reading] is 'if they were faced away' because alteration has no role in the occurrence of disjunction; perhaps it is a scribal corruption from the copyist.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage discusses the fourth branch: whether it is possible for all the atoms (jawahir) of the world to be separated and disjoined from one another such that no two atoms are in contact. One view permits this, drawing an analogy to a situation where all composition is removed. Another view denies it, arguing that contiguity (mujāwarah) among all atoms simultaneously is impossible, and separation (mubāyanah) requires at least the possibility of contiguity; if contiguity is impossible, separation cannot be validly ascribed. The author (al-Ījī) responds that it is sufficient for describing separation that contiguity is possible 'alternatively'—i.e., potential contact between any given pair is enough, not actual simultaneous contact among all. The impossibility of universal simultaneous contiguity does not negate the possibility of separation. The commentator then notes a textual issue: the phrase 'as if they were altered' (kamā idhā tabaddalat) is likely a scribal error; the correct reading should be 'if they were faced away' (idhā istadbarat), as alteration has no relevance to disjunction.

Key Terms
مباينة (mubāyanah, separation)
افتراق (iftirāq, disjunction)
اجتماع (ijtimā', conjunction)
مجاورة (mujāwarah, contiguity)
جواز (jawāz, permissibility)
تبدل (tabaddul, alteration)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Separation requires the impossibility of contiguity; if contiguity is possible, separation cannot occur.
Correction: Separation only requires the possibility of contiguity, not its impossibility. Even if contiguity is possible, separation can still be described as long as actual contact is not realized.

Checkpoint
What is the author's solution to the objection that universal separation is impossible because contiguity among all atoms cannot occur?
A) He agrees and denies universal separation
B) He says contiguity is not required for separation
C) He says that separation only requires the possibility of contiguity, not actual simultaneous contiguity
D) He says atoms are not real

Key Takeaway
For separation to be valid, it suffices that contiguity is possible between any two atoms; universal simultaneous contiguity is not required.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that separation does not require simultaneous contiguity, only the possibility of contact. How often do you assume that your current circumstances (health, wealth, relationships) are both necessary and permanent? What would change in your heart if you truly believed that every blessing is only possible and could be separated from you at any moment by God's will? Will you today remind yourself of this contingency and thank God for sustaining what you have?
?
The author corrects a misconception by showing that 'permissibility alternatively' is sufficient. In your own life, do you sometimes demand absolute guarantees before trusting God? Can you trust that God's permission for something (even if not realized simultaneously) is enough to rely on His wisdom? What is one area where you can practice reliance on God's possibilities rather than insisting on your own plan?
?
The discussion of atomistic separation might seem abstract, but it reflects a deep truth: all creation is composed of parts that God can separate and recombine at will. Does this reality make you feel anxious or liberated? How should it affect your attachment to material things and your hope in the afterlife? Commit to one concrete action today that shows you hold this world loosely.

The First View: Any Two Akwan Are Opposites
Al-Qadi's proof that any two akwan are opposites via a disjunctive syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reasoning behind the view that any two akwan are absolutely opposites, including the disjunctive syllogism used to prove it.

Core Idea
Two akwan cannot coexist because they are either similars or force two places.

This lesson examines the first position on akwan, held by al-Qadi and his followers. They argue that any two akwan are opposites due to a logical dilemma. Understanding their proof clarifies a key principle in kalam about the nature of place and opposition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim: any two akwan are opposites.
Read the passage where al-Qadi asserts that akwan are opposites. Note that they do not consider mumasah a kown.
This sets up the disjunction.
2
Analyze the disjunctive syllogism.
The proof considers two possibilities: either the two akwan specify one place or two places. Walk through each case and see why both lead to impossibility of coexistence.
The term 'similars' is crucial.
3
Identify the conclusion.
Since both cases prevent coexistence, the two akwan are necessarily opposites.
This is the foundation of the first view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد السادس : كل كونين متضادان
بالإيجاز الضابط لما هو مقتضى المقام ( واذكر ) أي أحفظ وتذكر ( هذا العذر ) الذي مهدناه لك هاهنا (لدي ما عسى تعثر عليه من قبيل هذه الأبحاث ( في غير هذا الموضع فتكف ( بالنصب على أنه جواب الأمر ) عني عني لأئمتك ) أي لومك .
المقصد السادس : كل كونين متضادان ]
يتصور
( من لم يجعل المماسة (كوناً قائماً بالجوهر كالقاضي وأتباعه (أطلق القول بتضاد الأكوان ) على معنى أن كل كونين فهما متضادان ) لأن الكونين المجتمعين فرضاً ( إما أن يوجبا تخصيص الجوهر بحيز واحد أو بحيزين والأول اجتماع المثلين) لأن كل واحد من الكونين مثل للآخر، والمثلان ضدان لا يجتمعان بل لا وجودهما في الجوهر إلا على سبيل التعاقب، كما إذا كان مستقراً في حيز واحد أكثر من زمان، فإن الكون المتجدد في الزمن الثاني مماثل للكون الموجود في الزمن الأول لقيام كل واحد منهما مقام في الآخر تخصيص الجوهر بذلك الحيز ( والثاني يوجب حصول الجوهر في آن واحد في حيزين فامتنع اجتماع الكونين مطلقاً فهما متضادان ( ومن (جعلها أي المماسة (كونا) مخصوصاً قائماً بالجوهر، وجوز قيام المماسات المتعددة بالجوهر الواحد ( كالشيخ والأستاذ فلم يجعلها ) أي الأكوان (أضداداً ولا مماثلة بل (مختلفة لجواز اجتماعها في جوهر واحد، قال الآمدي : والحق هو الأول أن المماسة المباينة اعتبارات موجبة للاختلاف في التسمية .

The Fourth Observatory - The Sixth Maqsad: Any two akwan are opposites. In brief, the controller of what is required by the context (and mention) i.e., memorize and recall (this excuse) which we prepared for you here (for what you may encounter of this kind of research (in other than this place, and suffice (nasb as being the answer to the command) for me, for me, against your imams) i.e., your blame. The Sixth Maqsad: Any two akwan are opposites. It is conceived (He who does not make mumasah a kown subsisting in the substance, like al-Qadi and his followers, (he pronounced the statement of opposition of the akwan) meaning that any two akwan are opposites) because the two akwan assumed to be combined (either necessitate the specification of the substance to one place or two places. The first is a meeting of similars) because each of the two akwan is a similar to the other, and similars are opposites that do not meet, but rather their existence in the substance is only in succession, as when it is settled in one place for more than a time, for the renewed kown in the second time is similar to the existing kown in the first time because each one takes the place of the other in specifying the substance to that place (and the second necessitates the existence of the substance at one moment in two places, so the meeting of the two akwan is absolutely prevented, so they are opposites. And he who (makes it i.e., mumasah a specific kown subsisting in the substance, and allows the subsistence of multiple mumasas in one substance (like al-Shaykh and al-Ustadh, then he does not make them i.e., the akwan (opposites nor similars, but different) because of the permissibility of their meeting in one substance. Al-Amidi said: The truth is the first, that mumasah and separation are considerations necessitating a difference in naming. Due to what preceded from his statement: (He did not make mumasah etc.) but rather he made it an accidental consideration to the kown of the substance in the place. His statement: He pronounced the statement etc., i.e., he said: the akwan necessitating the specification of substances to places are opposites, and he did not make the akwan into three divisions as will come. His statement: (similar to the other) due to their sharing in specifying substances to the place which is the last attribute of the kown. His statement: (opposites in the general sense) i.e., the two things that do not meet in one locus, whether they are similar or not. His statement: (a specific kown) other than the specific kown of the place. His statement: (opposites) he did not make the akwan absolutely opposites nor similar, but made them different like similitude and separation, for the akwan are different because of their meeting in the substance surrounded by the six substances. His statement: (and similars are opposites) i.e., like opposites in non-meeting, and applying the term 'opposites' to similars occurs in the speech of al-Amidi as well.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents a definition and proof concerning the opposition of akwan. It begins by framing the issue: the claim that any two akwan are opposites. Two positions are contrasted. The first position, attributed to al-Qadi and his followers who do not consider mumasah as a kown, holds that akwan are absolutely opposites. The reasoning is a disjunctive syllogism: if two akwan are supposed to coexist, they either necessitate the substance occupying one place or two places. If one place, then the two akwan are similars (since each equally specifies that place), and similars are opposites that cannot coexist; their existence is only sequential. If two places, then the substance would be in two places at once, which is impossible. Thus, in either case, the two akwan cannot coexist, so they are opposites. The second position, held by al-Shaykh and al-Ustadh who consider mumasah a specific kown, does not make akwan absolutely opposites or similars but different, because multiple mumasas can coexist in one substance. Al-Amidi then declares the first position correct, noting that mumasah and separation are mere considerations that cause a difference in naming, not in real opposition. The commentary that follows explains key phrases: 'he did not make mumasah a kown' means he considered it accidental to the kown of place; 'he pronounced absolutely' indicates he did not divide akwan into three types; 'similar means sharing specification of place'; 'opposites in the general sense' means any two things that do not meet in one locus, whether similar or not; 'specific kown' is distinct from the kown of place; 'he made them different' refers to the diversity of akwan like similitude and separation due to coexistence in a substance surrounded by six directions; and the application of 'opposites' to similars is found in al-Amidi's own usage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that similars can coexist because they are identical.
Correction: The passage clarifies that in kalam, similars are mutually exclusive in the same substrate.

Checkpoint
According to the first view (of al-Qadi), why are two akwan that would specify the same place considered opposites?
A) Because they are actually dissimilar.
B) Because they are similars and similars cannot coexist.
C) Because they would require the substance to be in two places at once.
D) Because mumasah prevents their coexistence.

Key Takeaway
The first view proves that any two akwan are opposites by showing that they can neither be coincident in one place nor in two places.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn that even the smallest movements (akwan) are governed by logical necessity and cannot coexist, how does this affect your perception of the consistency and precision of Allah's creation? Are you willing to let such rational proofs deepen your certainty in the order of the universe, or do you ignore the intellectual foundations of your faith?
?
This passage shows that a scholar accepted a conclusion based on a chain of reasoning, even when it might have seemed counterintuitive. Where in your own life do you need to submit to logical and scriptural evidence over your initial feelings or assumptions? What is one belief or practice you hold that you have not examined with such rigor?

The Second View and the Correct Conclusion
Second view (al-Shaykh, al-Ustadh) and al-Amidi's conclusion favoring the first view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second view that akwan are different rather than opposites, and why al-Amidi prefers the first view.

Core Idea
If mumasah is a distinct kown, akwan can coexist; but the first view remains correct.

This lesson presents the alternative view attributed to al-Shaykh and al-Ustadh, who consider mumasah a specific kown. They allow multiple mumasas to coexist, so akwan are not necessarily opposites. However, al-Amidi endorses the first view, stating that mumasah and separation are mere considerations causing a difference in naming. The passage also includes clarifying commentary on key terms.

Guided Path
1
Understand the second view.
Read how al-Shaykh and al-Ustadh differ: they treat mumasah as a specific kown and allow multiple mumasas in one substance, so akwan are not opposites or similars but different.
This challenges the absolute opposition.
2
Examine al-Amidi's conclusion.
Al-Amidi declares the first view correct, arguing that mumasah and separation are mere considerations that affect naming, not real attributes that change opposition.
This reinforces the first view's validity.
3
Review the explanatory notes.
The commentary explains why the first view's proponent did not divide akwan into three types, and why similars are considered opposites in a general sense.
This clarifies the conceptual framework.

According to al-Amidi, why is the first view correct?
